v
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Abstract
CAPEC Process Research Center
Department of Chemical and Biochemical Engineering
Master of Science
Simulation Tool Development and Control Study of Heat Integrated
Distillation
by Kristian Meyer
A generic model that can cover different distillation conﬁgurations is
presented and its solution procedure is analysed. The model is part of
the Bisgaard algorithm which may be used to study different distillation
column conﬁgurations. The algorithm was available as a simulink imple-
mentation. In this work, this implementation has been re-coded using the C
programming language and incorporated back into simulink as a C MEX
S-function. This effectively increased its execution speed with a factor of 80.
The Bisgaard algorithm is applied to a heat integrated distillation column
design adopted from the literature and its operation is studied. The column
includes a feed pre-heater, a reboiler, a condenser and a compressor. A
decentralized hierarchical control system consisting of a regulatory and a
supervisory control layer is designed using the LACEY procedure. The best
pairing of controlled and manipulated variables was determined as follows.
In the regulatory control layer the inventories in the reboiler and condenser
are controlled using the bottoms and distillate ﬂow rates, respectively, the
pressure in the rectifying and stripping sections are controlled using the
compressor and feed pre-heater, respectively, and a temperature differential
is controlled at the top of the column using the condenser. In the supervisory
control layer, two cascade controllers are used. The distillate composition
is controlled by manipulating the set point of a reﬂux ratio controller that
manipulates the reﬂux ﬂow rate. The bottoms composition is controlled
by manipulating the set point of a boilup ratio controller that manipulates
the reboiler duty. Interactions exists among all control loops, expect for the
inventory controllers. This decentralized system is benchmarked against a
supervisory model predictive control based system. The model predictive
control system coordinates control of the top and bottoms compositions,
using the set points of the temperature differential controller, both pressure
controllers and both ratio controllers. It is shown that the MPC controller
outperforms the decentralized PI-controller in different disturbance sce-
narios. The MPC controller is especially superior over the decentralized
PI-controller when logic is required to handle the disturbance scenario. Fur-
thermore, it is shown that both the decentralized and the centralized control
conﬁgurations are robust to measurement noise and a fully digital control
system working in discrete time is provided.

1
Chapter1
Introduction to Heat Integrated
Distillation
This chapter presents an introduction to heat integrated distillation and its
motivation. The objective and goals for this work is given along with a time
plan. At the end the structure of this thesis is explained.
1.1
Motivation
Distillation is the most popular separation technique for liquid mixtures
used in the chemical and petrochemical industry [1]. According to recent
estimates about 40% of energy involved in reﬁning, and other continuous
chemical processes in the US, is consumed in distillation [2] and this rep-
resented nearly 4% of the total energy consumption in the USA in 1988
[3]. The conventional distillation process is a mature process and a reliable
technology, that uses heat as the separating agent in a thermodynamically
inefﬁcient way, with reported efﬁciencies of below 10% [4–6]. Considering
the total energy consumption estimate, and the low thermodynamic efﬁ-
ciency of the conventional method, it is clear that the potential for energy
savings is huge within the ﬁeld of distillation. This has been realized and
addressed by theoreticians since the 30’s where Brugma [7] ﬁrst patented
an alternative design for separation of a ternary feed. However, it was ig-
nored by practitioners until the ﬁrst and second energy crisis in mid 1970s
and early 1980s, respectively, when the process industries were confronted
with the explosion of oil and energy prices [8]. Furthermore, greenhouse
gases such as carbon dioxide have risen in the earth’s atmosphere since the
industrial revolution and the earth’s resources are being depleted, which is
a growing concern for the health of our planet. These factors have urged the
interest in research in the ﬁeld, and as a result, extensive research has been
devoted over the last decades, to the development of more energy efﬁcient
and environmentally friendly distillation technologies.
==PAGE:20==
2
Chapter 1. Introduction to Heat Integrated Distillation
1.2
Background
Continuous distillation is a separation technique in which a feed mixture is
continuously separated into fractions by exploitation of the relative volatili-
ties of the components and removed continuously as output streams. These
fractions include atleast one fraction rich in the more volatile component,
and one fraction rich in the least volatile component. The fed may be su-
perheated vapour, a saturated vapor, a partially vaporized liquid-vapour
mixture, a saturated liquid or a sub-cooled liquid. In the following sections,
the conventional distillation column and two promising alternatives are
explained.
1.2.1
Conventional Distillation Column
The conventional distillation column (CDiC) is illustrated in ﬁgure 1.1A and
consist of a vertical column equipped with a heat exchanger (reboiler) at
the bottom, and a heat exchanger (condenser) at the top. To improve the
separation, the column is equipped with trays, or packed with a packing
material. In a trayed column, the liquid part of the feed entering the column
ﬂows down to the tray below due to gravity and the vapour rises to the
tray above due to a pressure gradient realized by the reboiler. At each
tray, the vapour from the tray below is condensed and mixed with both
the liquid from the tray above and liquid present at the tray (holdup). A
similar amount of liquid is vaporized due to the latent heat added by the
condensed vapour, that rises to the tray above. The section above the feed
stage is denoted the rectifying section, where the most volatile component is
enriched towards the top and the section below the feed stage is denoted the
stripping section, where the least volatile component is enriched towards the
bottom. To prevent loss of heat to the surroundings the column is thermally
insulated. The condenser may partly or totally liquefy the overhead vapour
product and return part of the liquid as the product and part of it is as
reﬂux to increase the separation efﬁciency. Similarily, the reboiler may partly
or totally vaporize the bottoms liquid product and return it to the bottom
of the column to generate the required pressure gradient for separation.
Because heat is added at the highest temperature and heat is removed at the
lowest temperature, integration of the utility streams from the condenser and
reboiler cannot be realized. In a technical review by Sulzer it is estimated that
about 95% of the energy needed for the reboiler leaves the system without
being used any further [9], resulting in a low thermodynamic efﬁciency.
1.2.2
Mechanical Vapour Recompression Column
The mechanical vapour recompression column (MVRC) is illustrated in
ﬁgure 1.1B, and was introduced in the 50’s by Freshwater [10]. The MVRC
provides a solution to the fundamental reason for the low thermodynamic
efﬁciency of the CDiC, which is, that one is unable to integrate the utility
streams of the reboiler and the condenser. The solution is to compress the
overhead vapour to upgrade its temperature and pressure to such a level
that it can be used as a heat source for the reboiler, where it releases its latent
heat and as a result condenses. Aftwards, it is throttled to the pressure level
of the column, where some of it might ﬂash into a vapour because of the
==PAGE:21==
1.2. Background
3
pressure reduction. The remaining vapours are totally or partly liquieﬁed in
the condenser, and part of the liquid is send back into the column as reﬂux.
This arrangement effectively increases the thermodynamic efﬁciency and as
a result reduces the energy consumption. This comes at the price of having
to invest in a compressor, which leads to a high investment cost. Thus, the
method have appeared to be economical only for close-boiling mixtures
where, because of a low temperature difference between the top and bottom
of the column, small compressor duties are required [6]. The MVRC has
been applied successfully in the industry for the separation of 1-Butene,
Chlorobenzene, ethanol, isopropanol and more, where energy reductions of
up to 80% has been reported [9].
1.2.3
Heat Integrated Distillation Column
The heat integrated distillation column (HIDiC) is illustrated in ﬁgure 1.1C,
and was introduced in the 70’s by Mah et al. [11]. In the HIDiC, the rectifying
section and the stripping section are separated into two columns. The two
columns are connected through a compressor, a throttle valve and a number
of heat exchangers. Hence, the two columns are heat integrated, and energy
is allowed to be transferred between the two sections. The heat transfer is
realized by operating the rectifying section at a higher pressure than the
stripping section, using the compressor and throttling valve to adjust the
pressure difference between the columns. The heat transfer between the two
columns results in gradual boilup along the stripping section, and gradual
condensation along the rectifying section. Ideally each stage is supplied with
enough heating or cooling needed for separation, such that a reboiler and
condenser becomes unnecessary, in which case the conﬁguration is denoted
an ideal HIDiC.
In comparison to the MVRC, the compressor of the HIDiC is placed at
the mid section of the column (above the feed stage) whereas the compressor
is placed at the top of the column in the MVRC. The theoretical advantage
of HIDiC over MVRC is that in a HIDiC the required temperature lift is
lower compared to the MVRC because the temperature is higher at the mid
section of the column compared to the top of the column. At the downside,
the HIDiC needs to compress more vapour, because the vapour ﬂow rate is
higher in the middle of the column, compared to the top. Thus, the HIDiC
needs to compress more vapour at a higher temperature. Therefore a HIDiC
should operate at a lower compression ratio, but not necessarily a lower
compression duty.
Several recent studies [12, 13] have shown, that for rare exceptions, the
MVRC design is typically superior over the HIDiC design with respect to
both energy and cost savings, but both designs outperform the conven-
tional distillation column, at least for mixtures with low relative volatilities.
Therefore, it is critical to benchmark the HIDiC against the MVRC and not
the CDiC. Although the HIDiC is an attractive energy efﬁcient distillation
technology, atleast for the separation of some mixtures, it poses great dif-
ﬁculties in realizing an effective conﬁguration in practice. Several studies
have proven the operability of the HIDiC by simulation [14–19] and experi-
mentally in bench-scale experiments [20]. However, the HIDiC concept has
not yet been implemented in industrial practice due to difﬁculties related
==PAGE:22==
4
Chapter 1. Introduction to Heat Integrated Distillation

1.3. Project Objective and Goals
5
with equipment design and lack of experimental data at sufﬁciently large
scale to prove the HIDiC principle [6].
1.3
Project Objective and Goals
The objective of this project is to use and develop a new distillation column
framework adobted from Bisgaard et al. [12]. The framework is a valuable
tool that can be used to compare the performance of different distillation
conﬁgurations by rigorous simulation and map the best for a given sepa-
ration. There exist a need to make the framework more accessible with a
simple distribution platform. In order to make the distribution worthwhile,
the use of the framework must be eased by for example providing a users
manual with clear examples. Furthermore, there exist a need to provide
more ﬂexibility with respect to different case studies such as high pressure
columns, which are interesting for the HIDiC principle, and to provide ﬂex-
ibility with respect to simulation time, to allow for example the study of
digital control systems which gives long simulation times due to sampling
times of the order of seconds. While the operability of the HIDiC has been
proven in the literature, there still exist a need to test the operation by rigor-
ous simulation. It is expected that the dynamic interactions are severe in the
HIDiC and therefore advanced control strategies such as model predictive
control (MPC) is expected to give good control. Implementation of MPC to
control the HIDiC have not yet been published in the literature.
1.4
Time Plan
The time plan including deadlines of assignments to hand in is given in the
following:
1. Introduction (deadline, 2. Feb). Write an introduction to heat inte-
grated distillation columns based on a literature study and identify
needs.
2. Model Description (deadline 9. Feb). Write a full description of the
dynamic model proposed by Bisgaard et al. [12] and analyse its degrees
of freedom. The purpose is to create an overview and understand the
method.
3. Bisgaard method and tool development (deadline 10. May) Write a
description of the Bisgaard method and apply it to obtain a steady-
state solution for a design given by the literature. Re-program the
model in C to accelerate the code and write a description.
4. Control Study (deadline 17. May). Using the accelerated code, per-
form a control study. Identify manipulated and controlled variables
and make a proper pairing. Test the performance of model predictive
control.
5. High Pressure Column (deadline 14. July) Identify what additions
to the current framework that are required to study high pressure
columns.

Chapter 1. Introduction to Heat Integrated Distillation
1.5
Organization of this Thesis
The outline of this thesis is as follows. In chapter 2 a rigorous distillation
column model is presented and the Bisgaard method is used to obtain a
steady state solution for a design adopted from the literature. In chapter 3
the model implementation in C is described and compared to the MATLAB
implementation. In chapter 4 a control study is conducted where a hierar-
chical control structure is designed from the bottom up using the LACEY
procedure. The use of model predictive control is evaluated and bench-
marked against a decentralized PI-controller. Finally, the work is concluded
in chapter 5.
==PAGE:25==
7
Chapter2
Rigorous Distillation Column
Model
This chapter presents a distillation column framework adopted from Bis-
gaard et al. [12] including derivations of model equations. Contributions to
the description of the model consist of a summary and discussion of model
assumptions followed by an analysis of the model equations and its solution
procedure using a systematic approach suggested by Gani et al. [21]. This
chapter also presents the workﬂow required to use the framework. Contri-
butions consist of a description of the extended Wang-Henke bubble point
method, which is also analysed using the method suggested by Gani et al.
[21], and a more detailed explanation of the Bisgaard method compared to
what is currently available. Finally, the Bisgaard method is used to simulate
a HIDiC design adopted from Nakaiwa et al. [22] at steady state.
2.1
Background
The distillation column framework by Bisgaard et al. [12] includes a state-
of-the-art rigorous model for trayed towers and allows for both static and
dynamic studies of different distillation column conﬁgurations including
the CDiC, the MVRC and the HIDiC. The framework has been created with
ﬂexibility in mind, and extensions to the current conﬁguration libraries
can be realized due to a generic formulation. The framework includes
performance indicators such as the second-law efﬁciency and reports capital-
and operational expenditures for evaluation of column performance. A
key advantage is that the framework provides a consistent and generalised
foundation for comparing different distillation column conﬁgurations. The
framework is created using MATLAB and an example simulation is available
without a users manual. Thus, initial work consisted of understanding the
program ﬂow.
2.2
Model Assumptions
The key model assumptions are:
A1 The liquid and vapour phases are perfectly mixed on each stage.
==PAGE:26==
8
Chapter 2. Rigorous Distillation Column Model
A2 The liquid and vapour leaving each plate are in thermal equilibrium.
A3 The vapour holdup is negligible compared to the liquid holdup on all
trays.
A4 The total liquid- and vapour holdup in the compressor/valve stage is
assumed constant.
A5 There occurs no vaporization and condensation in both the throttling
valve and the isentropic compressor.
A6 The vapour phase is ideal.
A7 The heat loss and thermal capacity of the column walls and internals
are neglected.
Assumption A1 and A2 are necessary to simplify the model. Models of
mixing are available but are very complex and include partial differential
equations. Similarly, plate efﬁciency models are available that take into
account that equilibrium may not be reached for each plate, but accurate
models are complex, especially for multicomponent mixtures. The accuracy
that is achieved by adopting such models, at the expense of the dimension
of the problem and computing time, was considered by Gani et al. [23] and
they decided to use the simplifying assumptions. Assumption A3 simpliﬁes
the energy balance equation signiﬁcantly, which saves computational time.
Gani et al. [23] mentions that this is often a good assumption, since in
most systems the vapour density is considerebly smaller than the liquid
density. Skogestad [24] notes that the assumption may be poor for volatile
components, for columns with high pressures and for cryogenic separations
where liquid density often is low [25]. As pressure increases, the difference
between vapour and liquid density decreases and vapour holdup becomes
more important. Choe and Luyben [26] indicated that the vapour holdup
may be neglected for columns operating at less than 10 bar. Assumption A4
and A5 are convenient as they simplify both the energy and mass balance
of the compressor/valve stage present in both the HIDiC and the MVRC.
Assumption A6 simpliﬁes the model because it allows the use of the ideal
gas law which is a simple equation of state. Other equations of state are
available that are applicable for non-ideal systems, but complicates the
solution procedure of the model equations. This is explained in more detail
later in section 2.13. Assumption A7 simpliﬁes the energy balance, but the
thermal lag arising from the column structure is ignored and typically one
predicts faster responses than are observed [27].
2.3
Conservation Equations
In this section the conservation principle is used to derive a dynamic model.
The principle says that
The rate of change of mass/energy within a speciﬁed control volume,
equals the rate of which mass/energy enters that control volume, minus
the rate at which mass/energy leaves.

2.4
Constitutive Equations
In the following sections a set of constitutive equations are given. While the
above conservation equations are valid for any system, the set of constitu-
tive equations will change depending on the system investigated. The set
presented represents a system that exhibits ideal behaviour in the gas phase,
while non-ideality of the liquid phase is accounted for using an activity
coefﬁcient model.
2.4.1
Vapour-liquid equilibrium
Assuming an ideal vapour phase and a negligible contribution from the
Poynting correction, the modiﬁed Raoult’s law states
yi,j = xi,jγi,jP sat

The activity coefﬁcients are determined by an activity coefﬁcient model.
The model should be chosen carefully depending on the system and its
prediction should be investigated. In general the activity coefﬁcient depends
on temperature and composition
γi,j = γi,j(Ti, xi,j)
(2.43)

2.4.4
Compressor Pressure Proﬁle
In the following an expression that relates the inlet pressure of the com-
pressor with the outlet pressure is derived. This derivation was not given
by Bisgaard et al. [12], only the ﬁnal result was given. The ﬁrst law of
thermodynamics states that
Qm + w = un −un+1
(2.61)
where un is the molar internal energy at the compressor outlet, un+1 is the
molar internal energy at the inlet, Qm is the molar energy transferred as
heat to the system and w is the molar work done on the system. For a
compressor the work can be described by two terms. The molar shaft work
wsh and the molar pressure-volume work ∆Pv. The process is assumed to
be adiabatic, meaning that no external heat is being added or removed from

2.4.6
Internal heat transfer
Figure 2.3 illustrates a stage-to-stage HIDiC type pairing with Nihx internally
heat-integrated stages. The index m ∈[1; Nihx] denotes the coupling of stage
rec(m) in the rectifying section and stage str(m) in the stripping section. For
internally heat integrated stages in the stripping section, the internal heat
transfer rate is expressed as
qstr(m) = UihxAihx,m
 Trec(m) −Tstr(m)

(2.83)
where Uihx is the overall heat transfer coefﬁcient and Aihx,m is the internal
heat transfer area of stage m. Heat is transferred from the rectifying section
to the stripping section, so
qrec(m) = −qstr(m)
(2.84)
For stages that are not heat integrated the internal heat transfer rate is
qi = 0,
i ̸= m
(2.85)
Note that a stage-to-stage type pairing is not the only way to pair heat
integrated stages. Chen et al. [28] suggested to use just three internal heat ex-
changers, and proposed a design method for determining size and location.
They applied the method to an ethylene/ethane and a benzene/toluene mix-
ture and showed that a comparable thermodynamic efﬁciency was obtained
compared with the stage-to-stage HIDiC type pairing, but at a lower capital
cost. It is also possible to have an asymmetric column, where the rectifying
section is larger than the stripping section or the stripping section is larger
than the rectifying section. In this case, only a part of the column is heat
integrated.
2.4.7
Tray hydraulics
In a tray distillation column, liquid enters a tray from the downcomer and
leaves by ﬂowing over the weir. Furthermore, vapour enters a tray as

partly condenses, and exchanges matter and energy. From the vapour phase,
a ﬂow, caused by a pressure gradient, leaves the tray into the above tray [12].
This is a rapid hydraulic phenomena and may therefore be assumed to reach
an instantaneous steady state determined by the state variables. As a result,
the relations describing the ﬂow can be expressed algebraically.
Liquid ﬂow can be described by the Francis weir formula [29], in which
the volumetric liquid ﬂow rate is proportional to the amount of liquid over
a weir to the power of 2/3, hence

2.6
Systematic Model Analysis Method
The systematic approach suggested by Gani et al. [21] for analysis of a model
is used in this work. The method consist of the following steps:
1. List the independent set of equations representing the model, and all
the variables found in the equations.
2. Determine the degrees of freedom (DOF) by subtracting the number
of equations from the number of variables. Based on the DOF, select
the variables that need to be speciﬁed and classify them.
3. Establish an ordered incidence matrix of equations and all variables
that are not speciﬁed. This is done visually by putting equations
in columns and variables in rows and putting an * on column-row
index where a variable appears in an equation. If the incidence matrix
shows a lower triangular form, then all the equations can be solved
==PAGE:42==
24
Chapter 2. Rigorous Distillation Column Model
sequentially, that is, one unknown for each equation. If this is not the
case, an iterative scheme must be applied.
The property relations given in table 2.1 are not counted as variables or
equations. Furthermore, The following constants are not counted: The pure
component parameters, Aj, Bj, Cj, Dj, Ej, the gravitational constant g, the
universal gas constant R and the pure component molecular weight MWj.
TABLE 2.1: Property relations that are not counted

2.7
Analysis of the Dynamic Model
In this section the systematic approach described in section 2.6 is used to
analyse the dynamic model presented in the previous sections. The method
is applied to the two-stage classiﬁcation individually. In the following
matrices are denoted using bold face with a double underline and vectors
are denoted in boldface with one underline. A new variable NT is introduced,
describing the total number of mixing trays.
2.7.1
Mixing Stages
Step 1.
Table 2.2 lists the independent equations and variables for a column includ-
ing only mixing stages. Note that Eq. (2.86) describing the liquid ﬂow rate
Li is valid only for trays, and is therefore subtracted by two representing
the reboiler and condenser stage. Similarly, Eq. (2.89) describing the vapour
ﬂow rate Vi is only valid for the reboiler and the trays, and is therefore
subtracted by one, representing the condenser. Note also that there are only
NS −1 liquid and vapour ﬂow rates, as the bottoms ﬂow rate LNS is given
by UNS and the overhead vapour ﬂow rate V1 is given by W1 as indicated in
the general countercurrent cascade shown in ﬁgure 2.2.

Speciﬁcation of the following 6 vectors each of length NT : T , W , U, F ,
hF and Q accounts for the ﬁrst term of 6NT variables in Eq. (2.97). Next,
speciﬁcation of the vectors CV
i and CL
i and the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1 accounts
for the second term of 2(NT −1) variables and speciﬁcation of the vector At
account for the third term of NT −2 variables. Further, speciﬁcation of the
two matrices z and M L accounts for the fourth term of 2NT NC variables
while speciﬁcation of the vector Aihx accounts for the ﬁfth term of Nihx
variables. Finally, speciﬁcation of the wire height HW and the overall heat
transfer coefﬁcient Uihx accounts for the last term.
The speciﬁed variables are now classiﬁed. The temperature vector T and
the holdup matrix M L includes the states, and must be initialised. The initial
values are computed using the Wang-Henke bubble point method described
in section 2.8. The feed conditions F , z and hF are given by the separation
formulation. The tray areas At, the internal heat exchange areas Aihx, the
wire height HW and the overall heat transfer coefﬁcient Uihx are ﬁxed by the
physical design of the distillation column. The dynamic constants CL
i and
CV
i depends on the liquid and vapour loadings, respectively. Therefore, in
order to determine these exactly an iterative scheme must be applied. In the
distillation column framework by Bisgaard et al. [12] they are approximated
as constants based on steady-state loadings. The remaining variables are the
vapour draw ﬂow rates W , the liquid draw ﬂow rates U, the external heat
transfer rates Q and the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1. These are adjustable parameters
that may be manipulated using valves. In practise, the external heat transfer
rates are adjusted by manipulating valves adjusting the cooling/heating
media to the heat exchangers.
Step 3.
An ordered incidence matrix for all equations and all variables minus those
speciﬁed is given in table 2.3. The incidence matrix shows that a lower trian-
gular form is achieved, meaning that the equations can be solved sequen-
tially. Note that the derivatives need to be integrated in order to determine
the states ML
i,j and Ti, and that the ODE system is coupled. Usually a stiff
integration routine is recommended. In the framework by Bisgaard et al.
[12] the matlabs ODE15s solver is used.

nonmixing stage n. All variables that are related to the inlet stream to the

denoted with subscript n −1, will be considered known by solving the
equations for mixing stages described in section 2.7.1. Therefore, these are
not counted as variables.

2.8
Wang-Henke Bubble Point Method
In this section the Wang-Henke bubble point method is extended to work
for distillation conﬁgurations including nonmixing stages. The method is
used in the distillation column framework to simulate a steady state starting
guess. The following description is a contribution.
2.8.1
Bubble Point Flash
The Wang-Henke method includes a bubble point calculation and is deﬁned
in the following. Given Pi and xi,j a bubble point ﬂash provides Ti and yi,j,
that is:
(Pi, xi,j) →(Ti, yi,j)
(2.106)
==PAGE:51==
2.8. Wang-Henke Bubble Point Method
33
Equation (2.45) gives an implicit equation that can be solved numerically
for Ti, using for example the MATLABs fzero built-in function. Once the
temperature is determined the mole fraction in the vapour phase is given by
the equilibrium relationship Eq. (2.44).
2.8.2
Conservation of Mass
Mixing Stage
Assuming steady state the component mass balance Eq. (2.1) becomes:
0 = Li−1xi−1,j + Vi+1yi+1,j + Fizi,j
−(Li + Ui)xi,j −(Vi + Wi)yi,j,
i = 1, . . . , Ns /∈nnm
(2.107)
A total mole balance over the cascade shown in ﬁgure 2.2 over stages 1
through i gives
Li = Vi+1 +
i
X
m=1
(Fm −Um −Wm),
i = 1, . . . , Ns /∈nnm
(2.108)
Equation (2.108) and Eq. (2.44) may be combined with Eq. (2.107) to elimi-
nate L and y to give a system of algebraic linear equations that can be solved
for xi,j by a Gaussian-elimination procedure. The result is the same as in the
original method and will not be repeated here. The derivation is given by,
for example Seader et al. [31, p. 381-382]. The ﬁnal result shows that
xi,j = xi,j{Fi, zi,j, Ki,j, Ui, Vi, Wi},
i = 1, . . . , Ns /∈nnm
(2.109)
Nonmixing Stage
The total mole balance for the compressor Eq. (2.10) remains the same. The
component mole balance Eq. (2.11) is given at steady state by
yn,j = yn+1,j
(2.110)
The total mole balance for the valve Eq. (2.6) and the component balance Eq.
(2.7) remains the same.
2.8.3

Condenser Duty
Assuming steady-state, applying i = 1 to the energy balance Eq. (2.14),
noting that V1 is non-existing according to the cascade shown in ﬁgure 2.2,
and isolating the condenser duty results in the following expression:
Q1 = (L1 + U1)hL

2.9
Analysis of the Wang-Henke Bubble-Point Method
The extended Wang-Henke bubble point method is analysed using the
method described in section 2.6 including both mixing and nonmixing
stages.
==PAGE:53==
2.9. Analysis of the Wang-Henke Bubble-Point Method
35
Step 1.
Table 2.7 lists all independent equations and variables of the extended Wang-
Henke bubble point method including both mixing and nonmixing stages.
All variables that are related to the inlet stream to the compressor denoted
with subscript n + 1 and the inlet stream to the valve denoted with subscript
n −1 are not counted, since these are included when counting variables for
the mixing stages.
Step 2.
From Table 2.7 the total number of equations is 2NnmNC +8Nnm +4NCNT +
3NT + 2(NT −2) + Nihx + 3 and the total number of variables is 2NnmNC +
10Nnm + 5NCNT + 9NT + 2(NT −1) + 2Nihx + 1 leading to the following
degrees of freedom:
DOF =

2NnmNC + 10Nnm + 5NCNT + 9NT + 2(NT −1) + 2Nihx + 1

−

2NnmNC + 8Nnm + 4NCNT + 3NT + 2(NT −2) + Nihx + 3

= 2Nnm + NCNT + 6NT + 2 + Nihx −2
= 2Nnm + NCNT + 6NT + Nihx
(2.118)
Meaning that 2Nnm + NCNT + 6NT + Nihx variables needs to be speciﬁed.
Deﬁne the vector of length Nnm,
Pnm =


P1
P2
...
PNnm


(2.119)
and deﬁne the vector of length NT
P =


P1
P2
...
PNT


(2.120)
Speciﬁcation of the pressures Pnm and the Nnm isentropic efﬁciencies ηis
accounts for the ﬁrst term in equation (2.118). Next, speciﬁcation of feed
compositions z accounts for the second term. Further, speciﬁcation of the
following 5 vectors of length NT : P , F , U, W , hF , the vector of length
NT −2: Q(2 : end −1) and the two scalars: the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1 and
the overall heat transfer coefﬁcient Uihx, accounts for the third term of 6
NT variables. Note that MATLAB notation has been used to indicate that
the condenser duty Q1 and the reboiler duty QNT are not speciﬁed. At last,
speciﬁcation of the vector Aihx of length Nihx accounts for the last term.
The speciﬁed variables are now classiﬁed. The pressures Pnm and P
are variables that may be controlled using different actuators such as the
condenser duty, the reboiler duty or the compressor power input. The
feed pressure PNF is known, and based on this pressure the steady-state
stage pressures may be approximated by assuming a constant pressure drop
==PAGE:54==
36
Chapter 2. Rigorous Distillation Column Model
across trays. The isentropic efﬁciencies ηis are ﬁxed by the design of the
compressors while the overall heat transfer coefﬁcient is ﬁxed by the design
of the column. The feed conditions F , hF and z are ﬁxed by the separation
formulation. The remaining variables U, W ,Q(2 : end −1) and L1 are
adjustable parameters that may be manipulated.
Step 3.
An ordered incidence matrix for all equations and all variables minus those
speciﬁed is given in table 2.8. The incidence matrix shows that a lower
triangular form is not achieved, meaning that the equations need to be
solved iteratively. The iterative scheme is initialised by an initial guess of
tear variables Ti and Vi. A simple initialisation is given by Seader et al.
[31], and they discuss how it inﬂuences the convergence. The tear variables
are adjusted until the following convergence criterion is satisﬁed within a
tolerance:
NS
X
i=1
Ti,new −Ti
Ti,new
2
+
NS
X
j=1
Vi,new −Vi
Vi,new

≤ϵ
(2.121)
where Ti,new is the temperature from the current iteration, Ti is the tempera-
ture from the last iteration, Vi,new is the vapour ﬂow rate from the current
iteration and Vi is the vapour ﬂow rate from the last iteration.

2.10. Reﬁning the Steady State Starting Guess

2.10
Reﬁning the Steady State Starting Guess
The steady state starting guess obtained by the extended Wange-Henke
bubble-point method is reﬁned to meet speciﬁed product compositions by
simultaneous correction the following constraints:
dML
i,j
dt
≤ϵ
(2.122)
dTi
dt ≤ϵ
(2.123)
ML
T,i ≤ϵ
(2.124)
Ln −Ln−1 ≤ϵ
(2.125)
Vn+1 −Vn ≤ϵ
(2.126)
Pi −Pi,spec ≤ϵ
(2.127)
xD −xD,spec ≤ϵ
(2.128)
xB −xB,spec ≤ϵ
(2.129)
where Pi,spec is the speciﬁed pressure, xD,spec is the speciﬁed distillate prod-
uct composition and xB,spec is the speciﬁed bottoms product composition.
The manipulated variables, L1, U, W , Q and E are adjusted until all con-
straints are respected. The MATLAB function fsolve is used to solve the
constraints simultaneously.
2.11
Bisgaard’s Method
In the work of Bisgaard et al. [12] an overview of the workﬂow required
to use the model framework is given. Figure 2.4 gives a more detailed
step-by-step explanation of the algorithm, and includes an overview of the
variables that are required to be speciﬁed. Furthermore, it includes decisions
to make if convergence fails, which might be of assistance for new users of
the framework.
Before initiating the algorithm, the following variables needs to be speci-
ﬁed: the feed conditions for all stages: F , hF , z, the liquid side draw ﬂow
rates for all stages U, the vapour draw ﬂow rates for all stages W , the heat
transfer rates for all stages expect for stage 1 and NS Q(2 : end −1), the
stage pressures P and Pnm, the area of all heat integrated stages Aihx, the
isentropic efﬁciencies of all compressors ηis, the overall heat transfer coef-
ﬁcient Uihx and the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1. A sample speciﬁcation is given in
section 2.12.
The ﬁrst step is to obtain a steady state starting guess by using the
Wang-Henke bubble-point method described in section 2.8. The steady
state starting guess may need to be reﬁned by adjusting the reﬂux ﬂow
rate L1 in order to help the convergence of step 4. For example, if one
speciﬁes a to high reﬂux ﬂow rate, the solution given by the Wang-Henke
BP method may be that the column end compositions are 100 % pure. This
requires an inﬁnite number of stages, and therefore convergence is likely
to fail in the step 4. Furthermore, the correction in step 4 is computational
heavy, and for large columns the computational time might be long. The
computational time can be reduced signiﬁcantly by reﬁning the steady state
==PAGE:58==
40
Chapter 2. Rigorous Distillation Column Model
starting guess. Thus, the initial speciﬁcation of the reﬂux ﬂow rate may
require iterations. The Wang-Henke BP method outputs the liquid ﬂow
rates for all stages L = [L1, L2, . . . , LNS−1]T , the vapour ﬂow rates for all
stages V = [V2, . . . , VNS]T , the temperatures of all stages T and Tnm, the
compressor duties for all compressors E, the condenser duty Q1 and the
reboiler duty QNS. According to table 2.6, the remaining variables that need
to be speciﬁed in order to solve the rigorous model includes the dynamic
constants CL and CV , the molar holdups M L and M V and the tray area
of all trays At.
The tray areas At are given by the physical design of the column. Based
on the ﬂow proﬁle obtained from the solution of the Wang-Henke BP method,
the dynamic parameters CL and CV are computed from Eq. (2.86) and
(2.89), respectively. The clear liquid height above the wire HoW needs to
be speciﬁed in order to determine CL. A value of about HoW = 1.20 is
recommended, indicating a clear liquid height of 20 % above the wire. Next,
the molar holdups M L(2 : end −1) are computed from Eq. (2.88), where
speciﬁcation of the wire height HW is required, and is given by the physical
design of the column. The condenser holdup MT,1 is computed from Eq.
(2.95) and the reboiler holdup MT,NS from Eq. (2.94). Here, speciﬁcation of
time constants τRbl and τCnd are required. A value of τRbl = τCnd = 5 min is
recommended. The vapour holdups in all compressors M V are computed
from Eq. (2.96), where speciﬁcation of time constants for all compressors
τCpr are required and a value of τCpr = 10 sec is recommended.
All degrees of freedom of the rigorous model has now been used and
a unique solution exists. The desired distillate composition xD,spec and
bottoms composition xB,spec are speciﬁed and the steady-state solution is
reﬁned by simultaneous correction of constraints given by Eq. (2.122)-(2.129).
If the method does not converge, the steady state starting guess is adjusted,
otherwise the dynamic parameters CL and CV are recomputed based on
the reﬁned steady state solution. This concludes the method and dynamic
evaluation may follow.

2.12
Case Study: Separation of Benzene/Toluene
In this section a case study is presented for the separation of a benzene/-
toluene mixture using a HIDiC. The benzene/toluene mixture has a normal
boiling point difference of about 31 K, which is outside the application range
for the HIDiC. Thus, the system serves as an illustrative example, but is not
really interesting from a real application point of view. The system is the
most widely studied in the HIDiC literature, and therefore a design is easy
to ﬁnd for this system.
A HIDiC design is adopbted from Nakaiwa et al. [22]. They obtained
the design by optimization of the total annual cost (TAC) with respect to the
total number of stages. Their method resulted in a HIDiC with 40 stages in
the rectifying section and 40 stages in the stripping section. In this work, a
reboiler and a condenser has been added to give more ﬂexibility with respect
to operation of the column. Furthermore, a feed pre-heater has been added
with a duty speciﬁed as 500 kW. The total number of stages then becomes
NS = 83 including the nonmixing stage which is placed above the feed
stage at stage 42. All stages in the rectifying and stripping section are heat
integrated, giving a total of Nihx = 40 heat integrated stages. The Bisgaard
algorithm is applied in the following. The ﬁrst step is speciﬁcations.
The feed to be separated consist of an equimolar mixture of benzene/-
toluene consisting of 50 % vapour at a pressure of PF = 1.013 bar and a ﬂow
rate of F = 83.3 mol/s. The feed is to be split into a distillate consisting of
99.5 % pure benzene and a bottoms of 99.5 % pure toluene. A ﬂash calcula-
tion determines the feed temperature as TF = 386.6 K and the enthalpy as

2.13
Discussion
One limitation of the presented model is that it assumes an ideal gas phase.
It has been postulated in a recent study by Harwardt et al. [13] that typically
MVRC designs are superior over HIDiC designs with respect to energy and
cost savings with rare exceptions. This postulate is in agreement with the
results obtained by Bisgaard [19]. One interesting case study is the separation
of a propylene/propane mixture, as this is one of the rare cases where it has
been shown that HIDiC outperforms MVRC and at the same time it is an
industrial application. In an operational and economic analysis by Schmal et
al. [32] it was shown that for the separation of a propylene/propane mixture
the HIDiC can save 14% in annualized cost compared to the MVRC (safe
estimate), but at the cost of operability. Therefore, it is of interest to study
the dynamics of the propylene/propane splitter. However, the gas phase
of the propylene/propane system cannot be described by the ideal gas law
and therefore rigorous equilibrium calculations are required. Furthermore,
the propylene/propane splitter is typically operated at high pressures, and
therefore the assumption of negligible vapour hold up is also not valid.
Bisgaard et al. [12] states that a system of differential-algebraic equations
(DAE) results if the ideal gas phase assumption is to be deleted, which
complicates the solution procedure. That is true atleast using the current
solution framework. The rigorous equilibrium constant is given by
Ki,j =
γi,jφL
i,j
φV
i,j
(2.133)
where φL
i,j is the pure liquid fugacity coefﬁcient of component j and φV
i,j is
the partial vapour fugacity of component j in the NC component mixture.
In general, we have that
γi,j = γi,j{Ti, xi,j}
(2.134)
φL
i,j = φL
i,j{Ti, Pi, xi,j}
(2.135)
φV
i,j = φV
i,j{Ti, Pi, yi,j}
(2.136)
Thus, in order to solve the rigorous VLE calculation the temperature Ti, the
pressure Pi and the mole fractions in the liquid xi,j and vapour yi,j phases
needs to be available. However, at each time step, only the temperature
Ti and liquid phase mole fraction xi,j are available from the state vector.
Therefore, one must extend the state vector to include also Pi and yi,j in
which case a DAE system results. However, there does exists another method
which makes it possible to include rigorous equilibrium calculations and
also include vapour holdups and still retain an ODE system.
==PAGE:64==
46
Chapter 2. Rigorous Distillation Column Model
The dynamic component balance for mixing trays including the vapour
holdup is given by
dNi,j
dt
= Li−1xi−1,j + Vi+1yi+1,j + Fizi,j
−(Li + Ui)xi,j −(Vi + Wi)yi,j,
i = 1, . . . , Ns /∈nnm
(2.137)
where Ni,j = ML
i,j + MV
i,j. The dynamic energy balance is
dZi
dt = Li−1hL
i−1 + Vi+1hV
i+1 + FihF,i + Qi + qi
−(Li + Ui)hL
i −(Vi + Wi)hV
i ,
i = 1, . . . , NS /∈nnm
(2.138)
where Zi = ML
T,iuL
i +MV
T,iuV
i . In the rigorous model presented in this chapter,
the vapour-liquid equilibrium was calculated from a simple Tx ﬂash, which
results due to the assumption of an ideal gas phase. The Tx-ﬂash is deﬁned
as follows: given the stage temperature Ti and the liquid mole fraction xi,j,
perform a Tx-ﬂash to obtain the stage pressure Pi, and the vapour mole
fraction yi,j:
(Ti, xi,j) →(Pi, yi,j)
(2.139)
When the vapour holdups are included, it is no longer convenient to trans-
form the state vector to temperature. Instead it is more convenient to keep
internal energy as a state variable, and then perform an UV -ﬂash. The UV -
ﬂash is deﬁned as follows: Given the internal energy Zi, the component
holdups Ni,j and the total tray volume V OLi, perform an UV -ﬂash to obtain
the stage temperature Ti, the stage pressure Pi, the liquid phase split ML
i,j,
the vapour phase split MV
i,j, the liquid phase composition xi,j and the vapour
phase composition yi,j. That is
(Zi, Ni,j, V OLi) →(Ti, Pi, ML
i,j, MV
i,j, xi,j, yi,j)
(2.140)
The total tray volume is given by the physical design of the column. Thus, the
solution procedure is exactly the same as the one presented in chapter 2, the
only difference being that one has to perform a more complicated UV -ﬂash
compared to the very simple Tx-ﬂash. The UV -ﬂash is more complicated
since it requires an iterative scheme to solve it. An effective scheme such
as the simpliﬁed ﬂash calculation for cubic equations of state suggested by
Michelsen [33] is expected to signiﬁcatly increase the computational time.
This solution procedure has been implemented in a recent study by Flatby et
al. [34] and applied to an industrial propane-butane conventional distillation
column operating at 13.2 bar. They showed that the molar vapour holdup is
about 40 % of that in the liquid and therefore the response was signiﬁcantly
faster when the vapour holdup was neglected. It was not possible to locate
any references where the above described procedure has been implemented
to model the HIDiC, but could consist of a very interesting future project.
The time limit during this project is limited, and therefore it was decided not
to study this procedure any further. Instead it is left as an open suggestion
for future development work.
==PAGE:65==
47
Chapter3
Implementation in C
The dynamic model described in chapter 2 is available as a MATLAB S-
function. This chapter presents a description of how this MATLAB imple-
mentation was re-coded using the C programming language.
3.1
Introduction
The dynamic model described in chapter 2 has been implemented in simulink
using the S-function block as a MATLAB S-function by Bisgaard et al. [12].
Figure 3.1 gives the simulink block diagram for the HIDiC described in
section 2.12 operated in open loop. From ﬁgure 2.5, the dynamic degrees of
freedom (manipulated variables) are the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1, the distillate
ﬂow rate D, the condenser duty QCnd, the bottoms ﬂow rate B, the reboiler
duty QRbl, the feed pre-heater duty QF and the power input E. Disturbance
variables are the feed ﬂow rate F, the feed composition z and the feed en-
thalpy hF . These are given as an input port to the S-function and nominal
values are obtained by applying the Bisgaard method described in chapter
2. The remaining degrees of freedom are ﬁxed and are speciﬁed inside the
S-function block as S-function parameters in a structure format.
The outputs from the S-function includes the end compositions xD and
xB. In principle compositions on all trays could be given as outputs but in
this study only the end compositions are measured. Furthermore, the model
outputs the holdup in the condenser MCnd, the holdup in the reboiler MRbl,
all stage temperatures Ti and all stage pressures Pi. The simulink select
block can then be used to select the trays at which temperature and pressure
are to be measured. In this work ratio control is to be applied, and therefore
the model outputs have been extended to include a measurement of the
reﬂux ﬂow rate L1, the distillate ﬂow rate D, the bottoms ﬂow rate B and the
boil-up ﬂow rate VNS. In the next section the motivation for implementing
the S-function in C is given.
3.2
Motivation
The motivation for implementing the MATLAB S-function in C is to provide
a tool that is ﬂexible with respect to simulation time. The tool is to be
distributed, and therefore it is expected that the tool is to be used for many
==PAGE:66==
48
Chapter 3. Implementation in C

FIGURE 3.1: Open loop dynamic model.
different case studies including complicated columns. Solution of the model
equations are computational heavy, especially for large columns including
multicomponent mixtures. Even for the simple column described in section
2.12 the simulation time becomes long once a complete control system is
implemented, which becomes clear at the end of chapter 4. The MATLAB S-
function is slow compared to the C MEX S-function because it is interpreted
by the MATLAB engine, whereas the C MEX S-function has been compiled
into native machine code that the host CPU reads directly.
3.3
C Code Description
The simulink S-function block provides the option of incorporating efﬁcient
C code into a simulink implementation. The input to the simulink S-function
block is either a MATLAB S-function or a C MEX S-function. The C MEX
S-function is a S-function written using the C programming language. In
order for simulink to compile the C code correctly, it must follow the S-
function application programming interface (API) syntax, which enables one
to interact with the simulink engine. The C code must include the following:
• The header ﬁle simstruc.h, as it deﬁnes the data structure that the
simulink engine uses.
• A number of callback methods. These gives information required by
the simulink engine. The MATLAB documentation gives a comprehen-
sive description of each of the mandatory callback methods.
• A S-function trailer at the end to indicate that the S-function has ended.
The C MEX S-function source code is provided in appendix A. It is built using
the same input structure as the MATLAB S-function. Therefore, it requires
==PAGE:67==
3.4. Usage
49
the same inputs saved in the same structure format. The C implementation
includes the use of some simple math operations such as pow from the
math.h header ﬁle. Furthermore, it uses the computers memory efﬁciently,
by initialising only the required variables to zero. Other variables, that are
not required to be initialised to zero, are initialised to the given number
located at the memory address that the variable points to. The following
example shows when a variable should be initialised to zero. Consider the
computation of a simple sum using the following C-syntax:
1
double x [ 2 ] = {0 ,
0}
2
for
( i n t
j =0;
j <2;
j ++) {
3
x [ j ] = x [ j ] + 2;
4
}
The x array is initialised to 0, so the ﬁrst step in the for loop becomes
x[0] = 0 + 2 = 2. Next we get x[1] = 2 + 2 = 4. Unexpected behaviour might
occur if the array is not initialised to zero, consider for example:
1
double x [ 2 ]
2
for
( i n t
j =0;
j <2;
j ++) {
3
x [ j ] = x [ j ] + 2;
4
}
The x array is now initialised to the given number located in the memory
address that it points to, for example, assume it is 10001. Then, the ﬁrst
step in the loop becomes: x[0] = 10001 + 2 = 10003. The next step becomes
x[1] = 10003 + 2 = 10005. This is obviously not desired.
A programming difference between the two implementations are ex-
plained in the following. Consider a matrix of liquid mole fractions stored
in MATLAB as a matrix:
xmat =
 x1,1
x1,2
x2,1
x2,2

In MATLAB one can get x1,1 and x1,2 using syntax such as xmat(1, 1) and
xmat(1, 2). In C it is more convenient to save the same matrix as a vector of
the form:
xC


x1,1
x2,1
x1,2
x2,2


Using C syntax one can get x1,1 and x1,2 as xC[0] and xC[2], respectively.
Note that the array starts at index 0 in C whereas it starts at index 1 in
MATLAB. Furthermore, square brackets are used in C whereas in MATLAB
parenthesis are used to index into vectors and matrices.
3.4
Usage
The C function needs to be compiled into a MATLAB executable (MEX)
before it can be used. The compiled MEX ﬁle depends on the operating
system of the host CPU and therefore it is recommended to compile it for
ones local machine. The MATLAB function mex can be used to compile the
==PAGE:68==
50
Chapter 3. Implementation in C
C function into a MEX ﬁle. A third-party C compiler supported by MATLAB
is required to be installed on the machine in order to use the mex function.
3.5
Validation
Writing code in a low level programming language such as C is generally
more error prone and debugging is more cumbersome. Therefore, it is
important to validate the C MEX S-function. Figure 3.2 shows the response
to a step change of 20 % in the feed ﬂow rate for the HIDiC separating
benzene/toluene described in section 2.12. The response from the C MEX S-
function is the same as the response from the MATLAB S-function. Therefore
it is concluded that the functions are equal, and the C MEX S-function may
be used for simulations. The dynamics of this response is explained in
chapter 4.

3.6
Speed Comparison
In this section, the simulation speed of the MATLAB S-function and the C
MEX S-functions is compared. The simulations are carried out on a macbook
pro with a 2,7 GHz Intel Core i5 processor and 8 GB DDR3 memory. First,
consider a digital control system working in the time scale of seconds. In
==PAGE:69==
3.6. Speed Comparison
51
order to simulate such a system, the continuous (analog) signal from the
model needs to be converted to a digital signal using an analog-to-digital
(A/D) converter. In this work, the A/D converter is simulated using the
zero-order-hold block from the simulink block library. A sample time of
TS = 1 sec is speciﬁed. The time required to produce the result shown in
ﬁgure 3.2, using A/D converters for all measurements, is 1.4 minutes for the
C MEX S-function function while it is 19.1 min for the original MATLAB
S-function. Thus, the C MEX S-function produced the same result about 14
times faster. That is a quite convenient time reduction, but it is certainly
possible to wait 19.1 minutes. At the end of chapter 4 it is shown that for a
full digital control system, the waiting time required to use the MATLAB S-
function is unrealistic and therefore the speed increase is not only convenient,
it is required to study digital control systems.
==PAGE:70==
==PAGE:71==
53
Chapter4
Control Study
4.1
Introduction
The operation of the HIDiC has already been extensively studied in the litera-
ture. Nakaiwa et al. [16] studied in 1998 as one of the ﬁrst the operation of an
ideal HIDiC seperating a benzene/toluene mixture. That is, a HIDiC with no
reboiler and no condenser but including a feed pre-heater. They used a sim-
pliﬁed stage model that neglects pressure dynamics developed by Huang,
Nakaiwa et al. [35] in 1996. Due to the use of a simpliﬁed model, only two
controlled variables are considered, which are the top and bottoms composi-
tions. In an ideal HIDiC only the feed pre-heater and the compressor power
input are available as actuators. Thus, there exist just two ways to pair the
input-outputs. They paired the top composition with the compressor power
input and the bottoms composition with the feed pre-heater duty. Then they
evaluated different internal model control strategies and concluded that a
nonlinear process model based approach was the best. In 1999, Nakaiwa
et al. [17] evaluated different control conﬁgurations for the operation of
a general HIDiC. That is, a HIDiC including a reboiler, a condenser and a
feed pre-heater. The addition of a reboiler and a condenser gives two more
control loops to consider which are the inventories in both. This gives rise to
a lot of possible control conﬁgurations that may be evaluated. They applied
the LACEY procedure to determine the best conﬁgurations and concluded
that a double ratio control scheme was the best in which the reﬂux ratio
L1/D controls the distillate composition xD, the boilup ratio VNS/B controls
the bottoms composition xB, and the inventories are controlled using the
distillate D and bottoms B ﬂow rates. Later in 1996, Huang, Nakaiwa et al.
[36] proposed a more rigorous HIDiC model, which includes the considera-
tion of vapour holdups and pressure dynamics [36]. They concluded that
the dynamic pressure distribution may give rise to a severe deterioration
on process controllability and should therefore be taken into account when
designing a HIDiC control system. Huang et al. [15] proposed a temperature
control scheme for the operation of an ideal HIDiC for the separation of a
benzene/toluene mixture. The main advantage of using tray temperatures
to infer the top and bottoms product compositions is that temperature sen-
sors are fast and reliable, whereas composition analysers might include long
dead times and expensive equipment. They demonstrated that the proposed
temperature control conﬁguration can retain stable operation around the
==PAGE:72==
54
Chapter 4. Control Study
vicinity of steady state. Liu et al. [18] designed a Wave model based Generic
Model Controller (WGMC) for the control of an ideal HIDiC separating a
benzene/toluene mixture and the WGMC showed superior performance
over other generic internal model controllers. In a recent study, Bisgaard
[19] designed a hierarchical control system based on rigorous simulation
using the model framework presented in chapter 2 for the separation of
a benzene/toluene mixture. The control system was designed using the
plantwide control system design procedure proposed by Skogestad [37] and
evaluated using Skogestad Internal Model Control (SIMC). Due to the inclu-
sion of pressure dynamics in the process model, the control conﬁguration
included pressure loops in both the rectifying and stripping sections.
In this work, a hierarchical control system is designed based on the
LACEY procedure [38] for the operation of the HIDiC described in section
2.12. The procedure is a ’brute-force’ method where all possible control
conﬁgurations are evaluated based on steady-state considerations. The
most promising ones are evaluated by dynamic simulations where each
control loop is designed using decentralized PI-controllers which are tuned
according to the SIMC procedure. The best conﬁguration is then chosen.
Using the best conﬁguration, supervisory linear model predictive control is
benchmarked against the decentralized PI-controller in both deterministic
and stochastic simulations.
4.2
Theory
The theory that has been applied to design and evaluate a control system
for the operation of the HIDiC is explained in this section. First the control
hierarchy is explained followed by an explanation of the LACEY procedure.
Three criterion for selecting the best trays at which to control temperature
is given. The proportional-integral control law is presented followed by
a tuning method for both singe-input single-output and multiple-input
multiple-output controllers. The model predictive control law is derived
and two metrics are deﬁned for the evaluation of the performance of the
controller output and input. Finally, the method used to obtain process
transfer functions is given.
4.2.1
Control Hierarchy
A typical control hierachy of a plantwide control structure is shown in ﬁgure
4.1. The ﬁgure is adobted from Minasidis et al. [39]. Each control layer is
described in the following starting from the top.
• Process optimization: The purpose of this layer is to ensure that set
points for the controlled variables at the lower level supervisory control
layer are optimal with respect to for example optimization of a steady
state cost function. In this work, this layer will not be considered.
Instead the nominal values obtained in section 2.12 will be considered
the optimal set points.
• Supervisory control layer: The purpose of this control layer is to
keep the controlled variables CV1 at the set points given by the above
layer CV1,s using as manipulated variables the setpoints CV2,s in the
==PAGE:73==
4.2. Theory
55
regulatory control layer and any unused manipulated variables. The
supervisory control layer may use advanced control strategies such
as model predictive control to deal with interactive processes and
constraints handling. The controller usually works in the time scale of
minutes.
• Regulatory control layer: The purpose of this control layer is to stabi-
lize the plant. Stabilization includes modes which are mathematically
unstable as well as slow modes that need to be stabilized from an
operator point of view. The regulatory control layer uses low complex-
ity controllers such as single-input-single-output (SISO) proportional-
integral controllers working at the time scale of seconds.
It is important to use different time scales between the control layers such
that for example the local controllers in the regulatory control layer has
enough time to realize their objectives given from the layer above.
FIGURE 4.1: Typical control hierachy adopted from [39].
4.2.2
Bottom-up Design Using the LACEY Procedure
The control hierarchy is designed from the bottom up starting with the
regulatory control layer followed by design of the supervisory control layer
as suggested by Skogestad [37]. Each control layer is designed using the
LACEY procedure [38], which consist of the following steps:
1. Select controlled variables. Use primarily engineering judgement
based on process understanding.
2. Select manipulated variables. Find the set of manipulated variables
that gives the largest Morari Resilience index (MRI) and the lowest
condition number (CN) of the steady-state open-loop process transfer
function matrix G.
==PAGE:74==
56
Chapter 4. Control Study
3. Eliminate unworkable variable pairings. Eliminate pairings with nega-
tive Niederlinski Index (NI).
4. Find the best pairing from the remaining sets. Tune the control loops
and conduct a dynamic evaluation of each set.
In the following, the MRI, the CN and the NI indices are explained. Fur-
thermore, the relative gain array (RGA) is explained, which is also used to
design the control structure.
Niederlinski Index
The Niederlinksi Index is given by
NI = det (K)
QN
j=1 Kjj
(4.1)
where K is the matrix of steady state gains from the process open loop
transfer function G and Kjj are the diagonal elements in the steady-state
gain matrix. The NI must be positive for stable control systems.
Morari Resiliency Index
The Morari Resiliency Index is given by the minimum singular value σmin
of the steady state open loop process transfer function G:
MRI = σmin
(4.2)
The magnitude of the index is a measure of the ability of the process to
handle disturbances, model-plant mismatch and changes in the operating
conditions. It depends on the controlled and manipulated variables, but it
does not depend on the pairing of these or on the tuning of the controllers.
The larger the value of the MRI, the more resilient the control structure is.
The minimum singular value is computed by singular value decomposition
using the MATLAB built-in function svd.
Condition Number
The condition number is given by the ratio of the maximum singular value
and the minimum singular value of the steady state open loop process
transfer function G:
CN = σmax
σmin
(4.3)
A large condition number indicates that the system will lack robustness. The
maximum singular value and the minimum singular value are determined
using the svd function.
Relative Gain Array
The steady state relative gain array [40] is deﬁned as
RGA = K ⊗(K−1)T
(4.4)
==PAGE:75==
4.2. Theory
57
where ⊗denotes the Schur product. The RGA provides a measure of the
interactions in the system. RGA elements of unity indicate no interactions
and for a 2 × 2 system a value of 0.5 indicates the strongest interactions
between control loops. It is recommended to pair RGA elements of unity
and one should avoid (if possible) pairing on negative steady-state RGA
elements. For a 2 × 2 system, the RGA matrix is identiﬁed from the ﬁrst
element RGA1,1 as:
RGA =

RGA1,1
1 −RGA1,1
1 −RGA1,1
RGA1,1

(4.5)
4.2.3
Criteria for Selecting Temperature Control Trays
In this work, temperature control of the column is to be implemented. The
issue of selecting the best tray for temperature control arises, and have been
extensively studied in the literature. The following criterion proposed by
Luyben [41] are used for the selection:
• Slope criterion: Select the tray where there are large changes in tem-
perature from tray to tray.
• Sensitivity criterion: Find the tray where there is the largest change in
temperature for a change in the manipulated variable.
• SVD criterion: Use singular value decomposition analysis.
Each of these are explained in the following.
Slope Criterion
Large temperature changes from tray to tray indicate a region where com-
positions of important components are changing. Therefore, maintaining a
tray temperature at this location should stabilize the composition.
Sensitivity Criterion
The tray with the largest temperature change is the most sensitive and is
selected to be controlled. A large gain indicates that the tray temperature
can be effectively controlled by the corresponding manipulated variable.
Choosing some of the potential manipulated variables in this analysis is
sufﬁcient. The temperature gain matrix KT is obtained by ﬁtting transfer
functions to the temperature response at each stage after the column has
been subjected to a 1 % step in a potential manipulated variable. Thus, the
matrix has dimensions NS × Nu, where Nu are the number of manipulated
variables.
Singular-Value Decomposition Analysis
In singular-value decomposition (SVD) analysis the steady state temperature
gain matrix KT is decomposed into three unique component matrices as
KT = UaΣVaT
(4.6)
==PAGE:76==
58
Chapter 4. Control Study
where Ua is an orthonormal matrix the columns of which are called the
left singular vectors, Va is an orthonormal matrix the columns of which
are called the right singular vectors and Σ is a diagonal matrix of scalars
with the singular values σ ordered in descending order. In this analysis
only the left singular vectors are used as these present tray sensitivities in
an orthogonal coordinate system. This coordinate system is such that the
ﬁrst left singular vector Ua(:, 1) indicates the vector direction that is most
responsive to changes in the manipulated variables. The second left singular
vector Ua(:, 2) indicates the next most responsive vector direction and so
on. The singular value decomposition of the steady state temperature gain
matrix is computed using the MATLAB function svd.
4.2.4
Proportional-Integral Control
This subsection presents the proportional-Integral (PI) control law in contin-
uous and discrete time. Furthermore, it explains the tuning method used in
this work to tune both single-input single-output (SISO) and multiple-input
multiple-output (MIMO) PI-controllers.
Control law
The objective of feedback control is the reduce an error signal to zero given
by
e(t) = ysp(t) −y(t)
(4.7)
where e(t) is the error signal, ysp is the output set point (reference) and
y(t) is the measured output (controlled output). The parallel form of the
proportional-integral (PI) control algorithm is given by
u(t) = u(t = 0) + Kc

e(t) + 1
τI
Z t
0
e(t∗)dt∗

(4.8)
where u(t) is the controller output (manipulated input), u(t = 0) is the
nominal control output, Kc is the controller gain and τI is the integral time.
Both Kc and τI are adjustable controller parameters that needs to be tuned.
For a digital controller, the control law needs to be discretized due to it
being a computer system. Let the sample time be Ts. Then the system is
sampled at a ﬁxed interval, as discrete time intervals deﬁned by
tk = t0 + kTs
k = 0, 1, 2, . . .
(4.9)
At these time instances, the output is measured using sensors and converted
to a digital signal using an analog-to-digital converter. The sensor data is
simulated using the presented model and the analog-to-digital converter is
simulated using the zero-order-hold block from the simulink block library,
as previously mentioned. In each interval tk < t < tk+1 the manipulated
variable u(t) is kept constant at the value uk, that is
u(t) = uk
tk < t < tk+1
(4.10)
==PAGE:77==
4.2. Theory
59
and is known as the zero-order-hold assumption. The discretized version of
the control law Eq. (4.8) is given by
uk = u0 + Kc

Single-Input Single-Output Tuning
In this work the Skogestad internal-model control (SIMC) procedure [42]
is used to design SISO controllers because of its simplicity. The procedure
consists of two main steps, which are (1) model reduction and (2) applying
the SIMC tuning rules. Step (1) consist of obtaining an approximate ﬁrst
order plus time delay model of the form
G(s) =
k
τ1s + 1e−θs
(4.13)
where G(s) is the process transfer function, k is the process gain, τ1 is the
process time constant, θ is the process time delay and s is the Laplace variable.
In this work, approximate ﬁrst order plus time delay models have been
approximated using a combination of the MATLAB function minreal and
Skogestads half-rule [42]. The function minreal is used ﬁrst to perform
zero-pole cancellation. Then Skogestads half rule [42] is used to approximate
the effective process delay, e.g. the process poles are approximated as a time
delay. After obtaining the approximate ﬁrst order plus time delay model the
SIMC tuning rules are applied and are summarized as
Kc = τ1
k
1
τc + θ
τI = min [τ1, 4(τc + θ)]
(4.14)
where the desired ﬁrst-order closed-loop response time τc is an adjustable
tuning parameter. The SIMC-rule for fast response with good robustness is
to choose τc = θ. If the process transfer function has a time delay of θ = 0
then τc needs to be determined by trial-and-error. Note that it is the initial
slope of the process response k/τ1 that determines the tuning parameters.
In this work the controller settings obtained for a continuous controller
is also utilized in the digital controllers. This is a fair assumption when the
sampling time Ts is small relative to the process time constant. Isermann
[43] noted that the continuous and digital controllers will have essentially
the same behaviour as long as Ts/τ1 ≤0.1, which is the case in this work.
==PAGE:78==
60
Chapter 4. Control Study
FT
RC
QCnd
L1

÷
RSP
FIGURE 4.2: In-correct ratio control design.
Multiple-Input Multiple-Output Tuning
For MIMO controllers the SISO tuning rules cannot be applied directly to
the design of decentralised control systems because of the existence of inter-
actions among control loops. In this work, the decentralized PI controller
is tuned using the sequential closing method introduced by Mayne [44]. In
the sequential closing method, each controller is designed sequentially. A
controller for a selected input-output pair is designed and closed. Then
a second pairing is selected and this controller is designed while the ﬁrst
controller is closed, and so on. Thus, the loop interactions from the closed
loops are taken into account sequentially. The method is simple because each
controller can be designed using SISO methods, such as the SIMC method
described above. A main disadvantage of this method is that the design
proceeds in an ad hoc manner. Design decisions are based on loops that have
already been closed which may have a damaging effect on the remaining
loops. Thus, the method depends strongly on which loop is designed ﬁrst,
and how this loop is designed. It is recommended to tune the fast loop ﬁrst.
4.2.5
Ratio Control
In this work, ratio control between two ﬂow rates is to be implemented. The
ratio controller manipulates one of the ﬂow rates in order to maintain the
ratio while the other ﬂow rate is uncontrolled (wild ﬂow). There are two
fundamentally different ways to design ratio controllers. The intuitive way
is to measure the two ﬂow rates that needs to be ratioed and divide the one
ﬂow with the other. The ratio is then given as a measured output to a ratio
controller that manipulates one of the ﬂows such that a constant ratio is
maintained. This design is shown in ﬁgure 4.2. However, it creates a highly
nonlinear control loop that does not work in practise, in which the process
gain is inversely related to the ﬂow rate of the wild ﬂow. In the second
method, the wild ﬂow is multiplied with the desired ratio to calculate the
set point for the controlled ﬂow. A ﬂow controller then controls the ﬂow
according to the given set point. This design is considered the correct design
and is shown in ﬁgure 4.3. Note that the set point to the ratio controller can
==PAGE:79==
4.2. Theory
61
FT
FC
QCnd
L1
D
FT
×
RSP
FIGURE 4.3: Correct ratio control design.
be used as a manipulated variable, resulting in cascade control structures.
Using ratios such as the reﬂux L1/D and boil-up VNS/B ratios for control is
an effective way to compensate for feed ﬂow rate disturbances when it is
combined with level control.
4.2.6
Model Predictive Control
In this subsection the model predictive control law is derived. Model predic-
tive control is a computer system and therefore sampled at discrete times as
for the digital PI controller. The model predictive controller will also assume
zero-order-hold. Vector and matrix notation using boldface with one and
two underlines, respectively, is neglected in this section.
System Identiﬁcation
The internal model used by the model predictive controller is a linear
discrete-time state-space model of the form:
xk+1 = Adxk + Bduk + Gdωk
yk = Cdxk + vk
(4.15)
where xk is a Nx-dimensional state vector, uk is a Nu-dimensional input
vector and yk is a Ny-dimensional vector of measured outputs. Ad, Bd, Cd
and Gd are time-invariant constant matrices. ωk is a Nx-dimensional vector
and vk is a Ny-dimensional vector of state noise and output measurement
noise, respectively. The noise is modelled as being identically independent
and normally distributed with zero-mean and covariance ιvw and ιwv. That
is
 ωk

[45].
Static Kalman Filter
A Kalman ﬁlter is used in the model predictive control algorithm to ﬁlter
the state and measurement noise. Using the ﬁltered state, the Kalman ﬁlter
predicts the future trajectory of the model outputs. It is assumed that the
initial state vector is normally distributed with mean x0 and covariance H0:
x0 ∼N(x0, H0)
(4.17)
It is assumed that the covariance H is constant as k goes to inﬁnity, that is
Hk+1|k →H for k →∞. Furthermore, it is assumed that ιwv = ιvw.Then

The model predictive controller is implemented in Simulink using the model
predictive control toolbox. A MPC object is created in MATLAB, in which
==PAGE:86==
68
Chapter 4. Control Study
the linear model, the tuning parameters and the constraints are speciﬁed.
The MPC object is then given as an input to the Simulink model predictive
control block. The toolbox provides a fast quadratic programming (QP)
solver using pre-compiled MEX functions. The speed that it provides is not
easy to match and is the main motivation for using it. The QP solver uses
the KWIK algorithm suggested by Schmid and Biegler [46].
Output Disturbance Model
Offset-free control is achieved by adding integrating disturbances to the
process model. The purpose of the additional disturbances is to delete plant-
model mismatch and/or unmodeled disturbances. The system is augmented
with an output disturbance model. Assume that the disturbance evolves as
ϑk+1 = ϑk + ζk

4.2.7
Controller Output and Input Performance
The output control performance is judged upon its capability of rejecting
unmeasured disturbances and rapid and smooth set-point tracking. The
integral of the absolute error (IAE) of the control error e = ysp −y is used as
a metric to quantify the performance and is deﬁned as
IAE =
Z ∞
0
| e | dt
(4.64)
The IAE is zero for perfect control. The MATLAB function trapz has been
used to approximate the IAE. To evaluate the manipulated input usage the
total variation (TV) is used and is deﬁned as
TV =
∞
X
i=1
| ui+1 −ui |
(4.65)
The TV should be as small as possible and is an indication of how smooth
the signal is.
==PAGE:87==
4.3. Process Equipment Capacity
69
4.2.8
Transfer Function Estimation
In this work process transfer functions are estimated based on step experi-
ments. The process data is obtained using the dynamic model. Two methods
have been used to estimate transfer function coefﬁcients from process data.
First a custom MATLAB function using a weighted nonlinear least squares
regression method adopted from Bisgaard [19] has been used. The built-in
function lsqnonlin is used to perform the nonlinear least squares regres-
sion. This custom function supports transfer functions up to third order
including one zero, that is
G = k
(s −z1)
(s −p1)(s −p2)(s −p3)
(4.66)
where z1 denotes a zero and p1, p2 and p3 denotes poles. In this work, the
function has been expanded to work for transfer functions including three
poles-two zeros and four poles-three zeros. The source code of the expanded
part of the function is given in appendix B.
The second method used in this work includes the use of the MATLAB
built-in function tfest, which uses a prediction error minimization (PEM)
approach to estimate transfer function coefﬁcients. The PEM approach
was introduced in the 60s by Åström et al. [47] for identiﬁcation of linear
dynamic systems based on process data. In general, the PEM approach
resulted in better ﬁts compared to the nonlinear least squares regression
method, and therefore it was decided to use this method. The function
tfest only accepts equally sampled process data. Here the accelerated C
code comes in handy. Process data have been sampled equally using the
zero-order-hold block (A/D converter) with a sample time of Ts = 1 sec.
4.2.9
Numerical Solver
The MATLABs ode15s solver has been used in all simulations. Numerical
difﬁculties was observed in some simulations. Therefore, it is recommended
to solve the model with a relative tolerance of atleast ϵ = 10−12.
4.3
Process Equipment Capacity
Before designing the control system the capacity of the process equipment
needs to be deﬁned. Table 4.1 gives the assumed limitations of the process
equipment. The rates at which the equipment can provide a control action
has been limited such that it takes atleast 1 minutes to go from the minimum
saturation limit to the upper. The rate limiter block has been used to limit
the rate of analog PI-controllers whereas the dynamic rate limiter block is
used for digital controllers.
4.4
Design of a Decentralized Regulatory Control Layer
In this section a decentralized lower level regulatory control layer consisting
of PI-controllers is designed for the operation of the HIDiC described in
section 2.12. The LACEY procedure is applied in the following sections.
