4.4.1
Dynamic Degrees of Freedom
The dynamic degrees of freedom equals the manipulated variables available
as actuators. From ﬁgure 2.5 the HIDiC has 7 dynamic degrees of freedom.
These are the distillate ﬂow rate D, the bottoms ﬂow rate B, the reﬂux ﬂow
rate L1, the condenser duty QCnd, the reboiler duty QRbl, the feed preheater
duty QF and the compressor power input E. Thus,
MV2 = D, L1, QCnd, E, QF , QRbl, B
(4.67)
4.4.2
Selection of Controlled Variables
In chapter 3 the open loop response to a 20 % step change in the feed ﬂow
rate was shown in Figure 3.2. The response shows that the holdup in both
the condenser and reboiler are integrating modes that must be stabilized.
In practise, there is also an integrating mode associated with the holdup at
the bottom of the rectifying section. This phenomena is not included in the
model and is therefore not considered. From an operator point of view, the
pressures in both the rectifying and stripping sections must be stabilized,
as these are slow modes drifting far away from their nominal values. In
addition, Skogestad [48] recommends to close a reasonably fast temperature
loop at the regulatory control layer because of many beneﬁts including:
1. Stabilization of the column composition proﬁle.
2. Indirect level control (reduces the need to control the holdups in the
condenser and reboiler).
3. Indirect composition control: strongly reduces disturbance sensitivity.
4. A remaining composition problem that is less interactive (in terms of
RGA).
5. Makes the column behave more linearly.
It is highly desirable to obtain indirect composition control because direct
composition measurements are typically expensive and introduces undesir-
able time-delay into the control loop, whereas the temperature measurement
is fast, reliable and inexpensive.
Due to the above mentioned beneﬁts, a temperature loop is included
at the regulatory control layer. One problem of temperature-based column
control is pressure variation. In the HIDiC, the pressure difference between
the rectifying and stripping sections are used as a driving force for separation
==PAGE:89==
4.4. Design of a Decentralized Regulatory Control Layer
71
and therefore pressure changes continuously in the HIDiC. Therefore, closing
a temperature loop including only one temperature measurement is unlikely
to give the desired composition control, due to composition being a function
of both temperature and pressure. This challenge can be overcome by
including multiple temperature measurements in the temperature control
loop. One of the simplest methods is to control a temperature difference
between two stages located in either the rectifying or stripping sections [15,
49–51]. For the rectifying section, the temperature is measured at the top of
the column where the composition is mainly one component, and at a tray
down the column where temperature is sensitive to changes in composition.
Similarly for the stripping section, the temperature differential is between
the bottom of the column and a tray up the column where temperature is
sensitive to changes in composition. If pressure affects the two temperatures
in the same way, then the temperature difference is unaffected [51]. In
practise, the effect of pressure on the two temperatures are not exactly the
same, however, this method is likely to circumvent mostly the effect of
pressure variation.
In summary, by applying engineering judgement, the following con-
trolled variables (CV) have been identiﬁed for the regulatory control layer:
CV2 = MCnd, MRbl, PRec, PStr, ∆T
(4.68)
The next problem arising is to determine at which stage the temperature
differential should be located. The tray at which temperature is sensitive
to changes in composition is determined in the following. Skogestad [48]
recommends to control temperature in the column end of the most valuable
component, because this minimizes composition variations in the important
end. According to ICIS (2015) the price of benzene is 1.04 $ kg−1 and the
price of toluene is 0.853 $ kg−1 [19]. Thus, the temperature differential is to
be located in the rectifying section. The criterion described in section 4.2.3
are used in the following to determine the single tray that is most sensitive
in the rectifying section.
Slope Criterion
The upper left graph in ﬁgure 4.4 gives the temperature proﬁle in the rectify-
ing section at steady-state conditions. The lower left graph in ﬁgure 4.4 gives
the temperature differences between adjacent trays Ti+1 −Ti. The location
of the tray with the largest slope is stage 13.
Sensitivity Criterion
The upper right graph in ﬁgure 4.4 gives the open loop gains between tray
temperatures and the following three potential manipulated variables: con-
denser duty QCnd, reboiler duty QRbl and compressor duty E. Perturbations
in the manipulated variables of 1 % has been used. The response is ex-
plained in the following. As the reboiler duty or the power input increases,
the temperature in the column increases, and therefore as expected, the
gains between tray temperatures and both reboiler duty and power input
are positive. The gains between tray temperatures and condenser duty are
unexpected, atleast from a CDiC point of view. As the condenser duty is
increased, one might expect the temperature to decrease because heat is

73
removed. However, for stages 7 until 17 an increased condenser duty results
in a positive gain. Figure 4.5 shows the response of the temperature on stage
12 after the HIDiC has been subjected to a 1 % increase in the condenser duty.
Initially the temperature decreases, but the ultimate response is positive,
that is an inverse response. Initially the temperature decreases because more
energy is being removed in the condenser. Stage 12 is heat integrated with
stage 53 which is far away from the condenser and therefore the increased
condenser duty has less effect on stage 53. Thus, the driving force for the
internal heat transfer rate increases which eventually results in an increased
temperature on stage 12. This phenomena indicates some of the dynamic
challenges associated with the operation of the HIDiC.
The gain curves show that stage 12 is sensitive to both condenser duty
and reboiler duty, while stage 13 is sensitive to compressor power input.
Singular Value Decomposition Analysis
The lower right graph in ﬁgure 4.4 gives the left singular values U1, U2 and
U3 from the SVD analysis. The maximum absolute values of the U vectors
indicate the locations of the principal components and these are the most
effective locations to place sensors [38]. The SVD results are contradictory
to the sensitivity results. The ﬁrst and second left singular value U1, U2
associated with condenser duty and reboiler duty, respectively, indicates
that stage 15 can be controlled both by condenser duty and reboiler duty.
The third left singular value U3 associated with power input indicates that
stage 9 can be controlled by power input.
Final Selection
While the three criterion gives contradictory results, the result does not
difference much. Later in section 4.4.3 it is shown that condenser duty is
to control the differential temperature. This was not known before hand,
and therefore iterations was required to arrive at the following selection.
A sensitivity analysis is conducted later in section 4.4.3 where the effect of
choosing different trays for temperature control is shown. It was decided to
use the results from the SVD analysis, that is, the differential temperature to
be controlled is ∆T = T2 −T15.
4.4.3
Selection of Manipulated Variables
The manipulated variables used for control at the regulatory control layer is
determined in the following. From Eq. (4.68) there are 5 controlled variables
and from Eq. (4.67) there are 7 manipulated variables available. That gives a
total number of
7!
5!(7 −5)! = 21
possible control schemes with
7!
(7 −5)! = 2520
permutations. The number of permutations is very large, and examination
of each is unrealistic. It can be reduced drastically by applying process
==PAGE:92==

Chapter 4. Control Study
TABLE 4.2: Possible control schemes for inventory control

knowledge and using only the pairing suggested by the RGA matrix. The
pair close rule suggested by Minasidis [39] is used and states that pairings
should be selected such that effective delays and loop interaction are mini-
mal, which are achieved by pairing controlled variables with manipulated
variables that are physically close to each other. The 5 × 5 control problem
is divided into a 2 × 2 control problem including the integrating modes
associated with the holdups in the condenser and reboiler and into a 3 × 3
control problem including the pressure in both the rectifying and stripping
sections and the differential temperature. First the 2×2 scheme is considered
and after closing it, step experiments can be conducted for identiﬁcation
of the open loop transfer function of the remaining 3 × 3 control problem.
Note that if the 2 × 2 control problem is in open loop one cannot conduct
step experiments because the system is mathematically unstable, whereas
the 3 × 3 control problem is unstable only from an operational point of view.
Inventory Control
Based on the pair close rule, the reboiler holdup MRbl can only to be paired
with either reboiler duty QRbl or the bottoms ﬂow rate B, and the condenser
holdup MCnd can only be paired with either reﬂux ﬂow rate L1 or the distil-
late ﬂow rate D. That gives a total of four different control conﬁgurations
and each are given in table 4.2. The process diagram for the (L1, QRbl) and
the (D, B) conﬁgurations are shown in ﬁgure 4.6 and 4.7, respectively. The
process diagram for the (L1, B) and (D, QRbl) conﬁgurations are not shown
but is a simple combination of the other two schemes. The (L1, QRbl) conﬁgu-
ration can be ruled out immediately because it includes high loop interaction
which is not in agreement with the pair close rule. To understand this, con-
sider that a positive step in the feed ﬂow rate is introduced in the system.
Then the holdup in both the reboiler and condenser increases. The reboiler
duty is increased to evaporate the increasing reboiler holdup and as a result
increases the vapour ﬂow rate up the column. At the same time, the reﬂux
ﬂow rate is increased to remove the holdup in the condenser increasing the
liquid ﬂow rate down the column. Thus, the two controllers work against
each other, introducing high loop interaction. The remaining three schemes
are considered in the following.
The effect of the bottoms ﬂow rate on the reboiler holdup, the effect of
distillate ﬂow rate on condenser holdup and the effect of reﬂux ﬂow rate on
the condenser holdup is derived analytically from a simple mass balance in
appendix C. The result shows that the effect is a pure integrator, that is

The large time delay associated with the reboilers effect on the reboiler
holdup is undesirable and therefore the (D, QRbl) conﬁguration is a bad
candidate for control. The (L1, B) and (D, B) conﬁgurations are investigated
by dynamic simulation. Figure 4.8 shows the closed loop response after the
feed has been subjected to a +1 % step in the ﬂow rate. The upper left graph
shows the condenser holdup and on the right the manipulated variable that
is used to control it. Note that for the (D, B) conﬁguration B is plotted on
the left y-axis and for the (L1, B) conﬁguration L1 is plotted on the right
y-axis. Similarly, the two graphs below gives the response of the reboiler
holdup with the manipulated variable on the right. As more feed enters the
column, the holdups in the reboiler and condenser increases. As a result, the
(L1, B) conﬁguration increases both the reﬂux and bottoms ﬂow rates and
the (D, B) conﬁguration increases both the distillate and bottoms ﬂow rates
in order to counteract the disturbance.
The ﬁgure also includes the response of the temperature differential, the
pressure in the rectifying (stage 2) and stripping sections (stage 43), and
the distillate and bottoms compositions. The open loop response is also
included. The response of the (D, B) conﬁguration is almost similar to
the open loop response, which is to be expected because the disturbance is
counteracted by increasing the ﬂow rates out of the column. The (L1, B)
conﬁguration response is not similar to the open loop response. As the reﬂux
ﬂow rate increases, the column temperature, compositions and the pressure
is affected. Thus, if the (L1, B) conﬁguration is to be used, the remaining
3 × 3 control problem is more interactive with the inventory control scheme
compared to using the (D, B) conﬁguration.
==PAGE:96==
78
Chapter 4. Control Study
Remaining Control Structures
The remaining control problem includes three controlled variables and ﬁve
manipulated variables. There exist one for each of the two inventory control
conﬁgurations (L1, B) and (D, B). Thus, there are
2 ×
5!
(5 −3)! = 20
remaining control conﬁgurations. Only the pairing suggested by the RGA
matrix will be considered, such that the number of permutations is also 20.
Thus, in total 20 control conﬁgurations are to be evaluated using steady-state
performance criterion. First, for each inventory control conﬁguration, the
open loop transfer function must be identiﬁed.
System Identiﬁcation
The process open loop transfer function for the remaining 3 × 3 control
problem is identiﬁed from step experiments after closing the (L1, B) and the
(D, B) conﬁgurations individually. Figure 4.9 shows the open loop response
with the (L1, B) conﬁguration closed after the column has been subjected
to 1 % step changes in each of the manipulated variables. Table 4.3 gives
the obtained transfer functions. The structure of the transfer function is
identiﬁed from ﬁgure 4.9. The upper left graph shows the response of the
pressure in the rectifying section after the column has been subjected to a
1 % step change in the condenser duty while the rest of the manipulated
variables are at their nominal values. This transfer function is denoted G1,1.
The next graph to the right shows the effect of the reboiler duty on the
pressure in the rectifying section. This transfer function is denoted G1,2 and
so on.
The open loop response is mostly as expected. In general, if more heat is
added to the system, the pressure and temperature increases, and if heat is
removed the pressure and temperature decreases. In the following some of
the responses are explained. First, the response of pressure in the stripping
section after the column has been subjected to a 1 % step change in the
power input (G2,3) is inverse. As the power input increases, the vapour ﬂow
rate increases due to a larger pressure gradient and as a result more liquid
condensates and ﬂows down the column into the stripping section. As more
liquid and less vapour enters the stripping section, both the pressure and the
temperature decreases, which explains the initial response. The temperature
in the rectifying section is increasing while the temperature in the stripping
section decreases, resulting in an increased internal heat transfer rate from
the rectifying section to the stripping section. Eventually enough heating is
supplied by internal heat transfer such that the pressure increases, which
explains why the ultimate response is increasing.
Next, the effect of increasing the distillate ﬂow rate on each of the mea-
sured outputs are explained. That is the transfer functions G1,4, G2,4 and
G3,4. As the distillate ﬂow rate increases, the condenser holdup decreases. To
counteract the disturbance, the control system decreases the reﬂux ﬂow rate.
Therefore, less liquid enters the column resulting in less vapour generation,
i.e. the pressure drops.

Figure 4.10 shows the open loop response with the (D, B) conﬁguration
closed after the column has been subjected to step changes of 1 % in each of
the manipulated variables. Table 4.3 gives the obtained transfer functions.
The responses that are different compared to when the (L1,B) conﬁguration
was closed are pointed out. First, the temperature differential response is
decreasing after the column has been subjected to a 1 % step change in both
the reboiler (G3,2) and feed pre-heater duty (G3,5) when using the (D,B) con-
ﬁguration whereas the temperature differential was for both cases increasing
when the (L1, B) conﬁguration was used. Note that in both cases, the col-
umn temperature increases as expected because heat is added. However,
for the (D, B) conﬁguration, the temperature at stage 15 increases faster
than the temperature at stage 2, and therefore the temperature differential
∆T = T2 −T15 decreases. As the reboiler duty increases, the temperature at
stage 15 is affected ﬁrst, and therefore it increases faster than the temperature
at stage 2. Similarly, as the condenser duty increases, the temperature at
stage 2 is affected ﬁrst and therefore decreases faster than the temperature at
stage 15. For the (L1,B) conﬁguration, the temperature at stage 2 increases
faster than the temperature at stage 15. As the reboiler duty increases, the
vapour ﬂow rate increases up the column resulting in an increased holdup in
the condenser. The control system counteracts the disturbance by increasing
the reﬂux ﬂow rate. This results in more vapour generation and the effect
is largest in the top of the column. As a result, the temperature at stage 2
increases faster than the temperature at stage 15.
Next, the response of the temperature differential after the column has

been subjected to a 1 % step change in the compressor duty (G3,3) is inverse.
As the compressor power input increases, the temperature at stage 15 is
ﬁrst affected, and therefore increases faster than the temperature at stage
2. Thus, the temperature differential increases, which explains the initial
response. As the temperature in the rectifying section increases, the internal
heat transfer rate to the stripping section increases because of an increased
temperature difference between the two sections and eventually due to the
increased internal heat transfer rate the temperature in the rectifying section
decreases. This explains why the ultimate response is negative. Note that
when the (L1, B) conﬁguration was used, the response was not inverse
because the increased compressor duty increases the condenser holdup and
the control system counteracts the disturbance by increasing the reﬂux ﬂow
rate, which increases the evaporation rate down the column. This results
in a hotter rectifying section and thus the internal heat transfer rate is not
enough to shift the temperature response.
At last, the response after the column has been subjected to a 1 % step
change in the reﬂux ﬂow rate is given by the transfer functions G1,4, G2,4 and
G3,4. The increased reﬂux ﬂow rate increases the internal ﬂow rates which
results in more evaporation down the column. As a result the pressure and
temperature in both sections increases.
Steady State performance Indices
In this section the 20 control conﬁgurations are evaluated based on steady
state performance indices computed based on the steady state open loop
process transfer function G. The steady state performance indices are di-
vided into two subgroups, one using the (D-B) conﬁguration and one using
the (L1-B) conﬁguration for inventory control.
Table 4.4 gives the steady state performance indices for the subgroup
using the (D-B) conﬁguration. The NI indices for control scheme No. (1), (5)-
(6) and (8)-(9) are negative indicating instability problems, and are therefore
not considered. Conﬁgurations No. (2)-(4) have high condition numbers
indicating inefﬁciency with respect to rejecting external disturbances and
are therefore not good choices. The remaining conﬁgurations are No. (7) and
(10) and these have almost the same CN and MRI indices. Conﬁguration

QF is used.
For both conﬁgurations, the RGA elements indicate severe loop inter-
actions. Furthermore, CN indices of about 100 are relatively high which
indicates difﬁculties in process operation. Conﬁguration No. (7) and (10) are
chosen as the best candidates of the ﬁrst subgroup and their performances
are to be evaluated by dynamic simulations.

for the regulatory control layer. It also seems intuitive that the (D, B) conﬁg-

less interactive as previously mentioned. Skogestad denotes this inventory
control scheme the ’standard conﬁguration’ and mentions that this conﬁgu-
ration will work for almost all columns (here he refers to the conventional
distillation column however) [48].

4.4.4
Dynamic Analysis
In this subsection both conﬁguration No. (7) and No. (10) are tuned and
evaluated dynamically in deterministic simulations. In the following conﬁg-
uration No. (7) is denoted the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration and No. (10) is
denoted the (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁguration as a reference to which manipulated
variables are used for control in the two conﬁgurations.
Tuning
The decentralised PI controllers for both the (QRbl, QCnd, E) and the (QF ,
QCnd, E) conﬁgurations are tuned using the sequential closing method de-
scribed in section 4.2.4. The ordered tuning sequence with the obtained
controller parameters for the (QRbl, QCnd, E) and (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁgu-
rations are given in table 4.6 and 4.7, respectively. The loops in both con-
ﬁgurations are closed in the same sequence. First the temperature loop is
designed with the fastest closed loop response time of τc = 200 sec for both
==PAGE:105==
4.4. Design of a Decentralized Regulatory Control Layer
87
conﬁgurations. This results in a tight temperature control loop that gives
indirect composition and pressure control. The temperature differential is
measured in the rectifying section, and therefore the pressure loop in the
rectifying section interactions faster with the temperature loop compared
to the stripping sections pressure loop. For this reason, the pressure loop in
the rectifying section needs to be detuned more than the pressure loop in
the stripping section which can be achieved but closing it as the last. Thus,
the pressure loop in the stripping section is closed as the second loop with a
closed loop time constants of τc = 700 for the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration
and τc = 1000 for the (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁguration. The closed loop time
constant of the (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁguration needs to be slower because the
feed pre-heater affects the temperature loop faster than the reboiler used in
the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration because it is physically closer. The pressure
loop in the rectifying section is closed as the last loop with a closed loop time
constant of τc = 2000 sec for the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration and τc = 7000
for the (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁguration. Again, the (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁguration
is detuned more because the actuators are physically closer to each other
and therefore affect each other faster.
An economical advantage of closing the pressure loop in the rectifying
section last is that the compressor then uses less control action because the
other two loops gets to work ﬁrst. It is much more economical to wear
down a control valve compared to wearing down a compressor which is
an expensive piece of equipment. Note that the controller gains are in
engineering units, which can be derived from the control law.
Dynamic Evaluation
Figure 4.13 shows the closed loop response after the column has been sub-
jected to a +20 % step change in the feed ﬂow rate for both the (QRbl, QCnd,
E) and the (QF , QCnd, E) conﬁgurations. Both conﬁgurations are able to
stabilize the column with the given tuning parameters. The temperature,
and pressure loops are tuned tightly because it is important that these loops
can follow set-points given from the above supervisory control layer. The
(QF , QCnd, E) conﬁguration controls the pressures in both sections slightly
slower compared to the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration because it had to be
detuned more because the interactions between the loops are occurring at a
faster time scale. The temperature loop is designed ﬁrst, and therefore this
has the least oscillations whereas the pressure loop in the rectifying section
is designed as the last and therefore has the most oscillations due to severe
loop interaction. Each loop affects the remaining loops, and therefore some
oscillation cannot be avoided using simple PI-controllers due to each loop
only considering its own objective. In this work the loop interactions at
the regulatory control layer has been dealt with by closing the controllers
sequentially and then detuning each controller, by trial-and-error, until the
oscillations are acceptable.
Figure 4.14 shows the effect on each loop of closing each of the remaining
loops. The upper graph shows that if only the stripping pressure loop is
closed, the control is smooth (blue line). However, closing the tempera-
ture and rectifying pressure control loops both individually and together
introduces oscillations in the system. The same is observed for both the
temperature and rectifying pressure control loops. This indicates that the

control loops affect each other, that is, interactions exists among them. Note
that the rectifying pressure loop becomes unstable if the stripping pressure
loop is not closed. Thus, if the stripping pressure loop fails to work, the
rectifying pressure loop must be taken off automatic by the operators. This
is a known disadvantage of using the sequential closing method. Each loop
depends strongly on the sequence that it was closed in, and therefore if
one of the remaining loops are left in open loop, then stability cannot be
guaranteed. Furthermore, the temperature loop becomes unstable if the
rectifying pressure loop fails. Due to severe interaction among the control
loops, stabilization of the HIDiC is not simple. However, the results shows
that stabilization is possible with acceptable oscillations.
Effect of the Temperature Loop
Previously it was stated that including a temperature loop at the regulatory
control layer would result in stabilization of the composition proﬁle while
also giving indirect composition control. Figure 4.15 gives the composition
response after the column has been subjected to a 1 % step change in the
feed ﬂow rate when the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration is closed and when
the temperature loop, in the same conﬁguration, is in open loop. Clearly,
the temperature loop gives both stabilization and indirect control of the
==PAGE:108==
90
Chapter 4. Control Study
FIGURE 4.14: Loop interaction analysis
==PAGE:109==

91
FIGURE 4.15: +1 % in F. The blue line is with the (QRbl,
QCnd, E) conﬁguration closed and the green dashed line is
when the temperature loop is in open loop.
composition. If the temperature loop is in open loop, both the bottoms
and tops compositions are slow modes which are considered unstable from
an operator point of view. This is one of the arguments Skogestad uses to
emphasise that a temperature control loop is important at the regulatory
control layer [48].
4.5
Design of a Decentralized Supervisory Control Layer
In this section a decentralized supervisory control layer consisting of PI-
controllers is designed for the supervision of the (QRbl, QCnd, E) and (QF ,
QCnd, E) regulatory control conﬁgurations. The LACEY procedure is applied
again in the following subsections.
4.5.1
Selection of Controlled Variables
In this work a dual-composition control scheme is considered. Thus, the
objective of the supervisory control layer is to control both end compositions
at their target values with no offset. Then,
CV1 = xD, xB

4.5. Design of a Decentralized Supervisory Control Layer
97
column, resulting in complicated responses. Therefore high order trans-
fer functions of up to ﬁfth order including complex poles and zeros are
necessary to describe the responses.
The open loop responses gives valuable information with respect to
interactiveness in the column. From ﬁgure 4.16 and 4.17 it can be seen
that a change in both the reﬂux ﬂow rate and the set point of the reﬂux
ratio controller, results in both the top and bottoms compositions being
affected with a similar magnitude. That indicates that the reﬂux ﬂow rate
can be used to control both the top and bottoms compositions, which is
rather unexpected, atleast from a CDiC point of view. In the HIDiC the
reﬂux ﬂow rate and the bottoms composition are closely related due to
heat integration of the column. If the reﬂux ﬂow rate is to be used as a
manipulated variable, it has to balance both purities, which implies that a
model predictive controller is needed. The same is observed for changes in
the set points to the temperature differential controller.
The rest of the manipulated variables has a larger effect on the bottoms
composition compared to the top composition. These variables include the
feed pre-heater duty QF , the reboiler duty QRbl and the set points to both the
stripping PStr,sp and rectifying PRec,sp sections pressure control loops. That
indicates that these may be used to control the bottoms composition and
that they are relatively decoupled from control of the top composition. One
might ﬁnd it unexpected that changing the pressure difference between the
two sections has a larger effect on the bottoms composition for the following
reason. As the pressure difference between the two sections are manipulated,
the internal ﬂow rates are affected, which has a similar effect on both the top
and bottoms composition. However, a temperature differential controller
is implemented in the top of the column, which gives indirect composition
control despite of pressure variations. As the temperature differential is
implemented at the top of the column, it controls the top composition better,
and therefore changing the pressure difference between the columns has a
larger effect on the bottoms composition.
Steady State Performance indices
In this subsection the 33 possible supervisory control schemes are evaluated
based on the open loop process transfer function G determined in the previ-
ous subsection. The steady state performance indices are divided into two
subgroups. The ﬁrst subgroup uses the (QRbl, QCnd, E) conﬁguration at the
regulatory control layer and the second subgroup uses the (QF, QCnd, E)
conﬁguration.
Table 4.10 gives the steady state performance indices for the ﬁrst sub-
group. The NI indices are positive for all conﬁgurations, indicating that all
conﬁgurations may be stable. The MRI and CN indices indicate that No.
(1), (5) and (10)-(11) lack the ability to counteract external disturbances and
are therefore not good candidates. Based on both the CN and MRI indices,
conﬁguration No. (3)-(4) and (6) should outperform the rest of the conﬁgu-
rations, which are No. (2), (5), (7)-(9) and (12)-(14). These three remaining
conﬁgurations are highlighted in boldface. The RGA matrix of conﬁguration

The suggested pairing is to control the top composition xD using the set
point of the temperature differential controller ∆Tsp and the bottoms com-
position xB using the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1. This pairing is not in agreement
with the pair close rule, as the bottoms composition is located at the bottom
of the column whereas the reﬂux ﬂow rate is located at the top. However, as
previously mentioned, the reﬂux ﬂow rate can be used to control the bottoms
composition because they are closely related through internal heat integra-
tion. Its MRI index is 10 times smaller compared to both conﬁguration No.
(3) and (4) indicating that it is less robust compared to these. Furthermore,
its RGA values indicate that this control scheme will be interactive. This is
not surprising, as the open loop response given in section 4.5.2 showed that
for changes in the set point of the temperature differential controller and in
the reﬂux ﬂow rate had a similar effect on the top and bottoms composition.
Thus, a model predictive control scheme will be required to handle the inter-
actions in this conﬁguration. Therefore, this pairing will not be considered
any further. That leaves conﬁguration No. (3) and (4). The RGA matrix of

Here, the top composition xD is controlled using the set point of the reﬂux
ratio controller (L1/D)sp and the bottoms composition xB is controlled using
the set point of the rectifying pressure control loop PRec,sp. The difference
between conﬁguration No. (3) and (4) is whether the bottoms composition
should be controlled using the set point of the rectifying or the stripping
sections pressure control loop. Based on the pair close rule, conﬁguration
No. (3) is better than No. (4) because the stripping pressure control loop
is located closer to the bottoms composition compared to the rectifying
pressure control loop. However, in the HIDiC, it is the pressure difference
between the two columns that determines the separation, and therefore it
does not matter if the rectifying pressure loop is far away, as it will not
introduce a time lag. Conﬁguration No. (4) has RGA elements closer to one
compared to No. (3) indicating a less interactive control scheme. Therefore,
No. (4) is chosen as the candidate of these two conﬁgurations and will be
evaluated dynamically. It is expected that these two conﬁgurations are very
much alike. The process diagram for conﬁguration No. (4) is shown in ﬁgure
4.18.

In total, conﬁguration No. (4), (21) and (29) are to be evaluated dynam-

conﬁguration No. (4), (21) and (29) are 1.04, -0.001 and 1.014, respectively,
indicating that the 2 × 2 composition control problem is non-interactive
at steady state for each of the three conﬁgurations. However, each of the
control schemes include the use of the set point to the reﬂux ratio controller.
The open loop response given in section 4.5.2 indicates that manipulating
the set point of the reﬂux ratio controller affects both the top and bottoms
composition with a similar magnitude. Therefore, some loop interaction is
to be expected for dynamic reasons.
4.5.3
Dynamic Analysis
In this subsection, the supervisory control conﬁgurations No. (4), (21)
and (29) are tuned and evaluated dynamically in deterministic simulations.
Based on the dynamic response, the superior pairing is selected.

uration No. (4), (21) and (29), respectively. In this work, the actuator valves
are assumed perfect. Therefore, the ﬂow controller that controls the reﬂux

ﬂow rate using the reﬂux ﬂow rate is given by a unity step. In order for
the cascade controllers to function properly, the secondary (slave) controller
needs to be tuned faster than the primary (master) controller. Therefore, both
the reﬂux and boilup ﬂow controllers have been tuned tightly with a closed
loop response time of τc = 1 sec, which ensures good set point tracking.
In all three conﬁgurations, the reﬂux ﬂow controller is closed ﬁrst, fol-
lowed by closing of the top composition control loop with a closed loop time
constant of τc = 10000. The top composition control loop is designed ﬁrst as
control of the top composition is considered the most important. Next, for
conﬁguration No. (21), the boilup ﬂow rate controller is closed, followed by
closing of the bottoms composition loop with a closed loop time constant
of τc = 30000. In conﬁguration No. (4) and (29) the bottoms composition
control loop is closed directly after the top composition control loop is closed.
The bottoms composition loop was detuned until loop interaction with the
top composition loop was acceptable. The same closed loop time constants
have been used for all three conﬁgurations in order to give a fair comparison.

Dynamic Evaluation
The closed loop performance of the three conﬁgurations are evaluated in
deterministic simulations with respect to both disturbance rejection and set
point tracking. Analog PI-controllers have been used in these simulations
==PAGE:123==
4.5. Design of a Decentralized Supervisory Control Layer
105
and upper saturation limits have been ignored. The following scenario takes
place:
t ≥0: +20 % in F.
t ≥20h: +10 % in zF .
t ≥40h: +10 % in hF .
t ≥60h: Increase xD from 0.995 to 0.996
t ≥80h: Increase xB from 0.005 to 0.006
The scenario has been chosen such that the system gets to reach a nominal
state before a new disturbance or set point change occurs. Figure 4.21
gives the closed loop responses. The controlled and manipulated variables
are shown in pairs. The top graph gives the top composition response
with its corresponding actuator, the reﬂux ﬂow rate L1, below. The third
graph from the top gives the bottoms composition with its corresponding
actuators below and so on. Note that the actuators for control of the bottoms
composition are plotted on two graphs because the three conﬁgurations
used different actuators.
The top graph shows that the three conﬁgurations can reject disturbances
and track new set points for the top composition without off-set in a similar
way. The similarity is supported by the IAE and TV given in table 4.15 which
are all of the same magnitude. The dynamics of the column are slow and it
takes about 15 hours to stabilize the top composition after a disturbance has
been introduced. Loop interaction was observed between all loops and as a
result the control includes modest oscillations.
The third graph from the top gives the response of the bottoms composi-
tion. The three conﬁgurations can also reject disturbances and track new set
points for the bottoms composition. The green line represents conﬁguration
No. (4) and rates the worst at controlling the bottoms composition. It is
especially not good at rejecting the disturbance in the feed composition
introduced after 40 hours as it takes a long time to stabilize the composition.
It also struggles when a disturbance in the feed enthalpy is introduced at
60 hours. It is to be expected that conﬁguration No. (4) is the worst at
rejecting disturbances in the feed enthalpy, because the other two conﬁgura-
tions uses a feed pre-heater and therefore removes the disturbance before
it is introduced in the column. However, it does have some effect on the
other two conﬁgurations, even though a feed pre-heater is available. This
is because the feed pre-heater is modelled as heat added to the feed plate
after the feed has entered the column, whereas in practise the heat is added
before the column. Therefore, in practise the effect of disturbances in the
feed enthalpy for both conﬁguration No. (21) and (29) should not be as
large. Conﬁguration No. (21) shows the best performance in controlling the
bottoms composition. From table 4.15 it has a slightly lower IAE compared
to conﬁguration No. (29). Note that the TV cannot be compared because
conﬁguration No. (21) uses the reboiler duty indirectly whereas conﬁgura-
tion No. (4) and (29) uses the compressor indirectly. Further, conﬁguration
No (21) rejects the disturbance in the feed ﬂow rate introduced at t = 0 more
smoothly compared to the other two conﬁgurations. This is to be expected,
as conﬁguration No. (21) compensates for disturbances in the feed ﬂow rate

by controlling the boil-up ratio which is a special form of feedforward con-
trol when combined with level control. Furthermore, conﬁguration No. (21)
uses the actuators in a more desirable way. Conﬁguration No. (4) and (29)
both control the bottoms composition by manipulating the set point of the
rectifying pressure control loop. The pressure is realized by manipulating
the compressor power input. Therefore, both conﬁguration No. (4) and (29)
uses more compressor control action compared to conﬁguration No. (21)
and also requires a larger compressor. In conﬁguration no. (21) the objective
of the compressor is to stabilize the pressure in the rectifying section and
instead rely on the reboiler duty to control the bottoms composition. Com-
pressors are expensive, especially large ones, and therefore it is desirable to
rely on the reboiler duty instead. It seems intuitive that conﬁguration No.
(21) is the best because it utilizes all the dynamic degrees of freedom and
compensates for feed ﬂow rate disturbances by controlling both the boilup
and reﬂux ratio. In conﬁguration No. (4) the feed pre-heater is unused, and
in conﬁguration No. (29) the reboiler duty is unused. Nakaiwa et al. [17]
also concluded that a double ratio control scheme was the best for operation
of a HIDiC separating Benzene/Toluene, they did however not include all
control loops as has been done here.

4.6. Supervisory Model Predictive Control
109
4.6
Supervisory Model Predictive Control
In this section a model predictive control based supervisory control scheme
is benchmarked against a decentralized PI-controller using the pairing of
control conﬁguration No. (21) which was determined as the best for a
decentralized system in the previous section. The closed loop performance
is evaluated based on deterministic and stochastic simulations.
4.6.1
Motivation
The motivation for implementing an advanced control strategy such as a
supervisory based model predictive controller for operation of the HIDiC
is that coordination of the control loops might improve the control perfor-
mance. One disadvantage of using a decentralized system is that it does
not effectively handle interactions among control loops. In section 4.5.2 the
open loop composition responses were discussed. From this discussion it is
clear that the HIDiC is an interactive process. For example, both the reﬂux
ﬂow rate and set point of the temperature differential controller has a similar
effect on both the top and bottoms composition, indicating that a model
predictive controller will be required to balance both purities. Furthermore,
each of the manipulated variables available has an effect on both the top
and bottoms composition. For example, in a decentralized system using
the pairing of conﬁguration No. (21), it is only the reboiler that controls the
bottoms composition. However, the pressure difference between the two sec-
tions could also be manipulated simultaneously to ’help’ the reboiler. That
implies that a model predictive controller is needed. Therefore, one might
expect that a coordinated control system can outperform an uncoordinated
one.
4.6.2
Objective
The objective of the model predictive controller is to supervise the lower level
regulatory control layer consisting of PI loops. All the set points of the PI
loops are available to the model predictive controller, which coordinates the
control action by minimization of the cost function given in Eq. (4.27). In this
work, all the set points, expect for those of the inventory control loops, will
be used. The level controllers have no interaction with the remaining control
problem and therefore only complicates the tuning of the model predictive
controller. Note that it is desired to make the model predictive controller as
simple as possible because more variables complicates the tuning. However,
it has been found that all the control loops are interactive, and therefore
performance is lost if any are left out. The manipulated variables are given
by
MVmpc =
L1
D

sp
,
VNS
B

sp
, PRec,sp, PStr,sp, ∆Tsp,
(4.84)
and the variables to be controlled are
CVmpc = xD, xB, PRec, ∆T, PStr
(4.85)
The process diagram is shown in ﬁgure 4.22. Note that the lower level regu-
latory control layer works as a backup if the model predictive controller fails

to provide a solution. In case this happens, the lower level regulatory control
layer still have the last set-points they received from the model predictive
controller before it failed and the process remains stable. Therefore, this
way of implementing the model predictive controller is more failure tolerant
compared to if the model predictive controller was implemented directly on
the process.
4.6.3
System Identiﬁcation
Figure 4.23 shows the open loop process responses (regulatory control layer
is closed) and table 4.16 gives the obtained transfer functions. Complicated
responses are observed, and as a result transfer functions of high order
are obtained including complex zeros and poles. These dynamics gives
an indication of the difﬁculties related to operation of the heat integrated
distillation column.
From the open loop process transfer function a linear state-space model
in discrete time is realized using a balanced realization. The sample time is
==PAGE:129==
4.6. Supervisory Model Predictive Control
111
speciﬁed as Ts = 3×60 sec. Here it is assumed that a composition analysis is
available every 3 × 60 sec, which is considered reasonable. Smith [52] states
that for a natural gas sample, a chromatograph can produce a composition
analysis in 15 seconds whereas it can take up to 15 minutes for a sample of a
heavy oil. An analysis of Benzene/Toluene is expected to be between these
two extremes.
Figure 4.24 shows the process open loop response to step changes of 1 %,
5 % and 10 % in each of the manipulated variables. For a linear system the
normalised step response is independent of the magnitude of the step change
due to the superposition principle. The graph shows that the response for
both the top and bottoms compositions are non-linear with respect to some
of the manipulated variables. This non-linear effect is not included in the
linear model. However, the non-linearity seems to be modest and linear
model predictive control should be sufﬁcient. Furthermore, because the
regulatory control layer is in closed loop, the process should not deviate
much from the nominal state.

4.6.4
Tuning
In this section the model predictive controller is tuned. The prediction
horizon is chosen based on a rule of thumb suggested by Wojsznis et al.
[53]. It says that the prediction horizon should be set so large that further
increment has no signiﬁcant effect. Note that as the prediction horizon
increases, the computational load also increases and therefore it cannot
be set to high either. The prediction horizon was set to 200 sample times
NP = 200 with a control horizon of 10 samples. That is, over the ﬁrst 10
steps in the prediction horizon a new optimal solution is computed but
from steps 11 until 200 the optimal input remains constant as ˆuk+10|k. Again,
specifying the control horizon as high as possible gives the best performance,
but increases the computational load. With a sample time of 3 minutes, the
prediction horizon is 200×3 = 600 min = 10 hours, which seems reasonable
considering the open loop process time constants which are derived from

sluggish. Based on the response, the weights are decreased if the response is
too sluggish and increased if the response is to aggressive by a factor of 10.
The weights have been tuned such that the local PI-controllers can follow
the set points. The weights of the temperature differential and the rectifying
and stripping sections pressure loops have been tuned faster because these
PI-controllers needs faster supervision, otherwise the local PI-controllers
cannot follow the set points given by the MPC.

The reﬂux ratio is constrained to ensure that product leaves the column,
e.g. at L1 = ∞the distillate ﬂow rate is D = 0 which is not desirable.
Furthermore, usually there is a maximum constraint on the reﬂux ratio such
that the column does not ﬂood, however, such constraints has not been
investigated in this work. The boil-up ratio has been constrained such that
the local PI-controller respects the upper saturation limit of the reboiler duty.
The input rate-of-movement is left unconstrained.
4.6.5
Deterministic Simulation
The model predictive controller is benchmarked against the decentralized PI
controller in a deterministic simulation. The controllers are exposed to the
previously described disturbance scenario. Analog PI-controllers are used
in the regulatory control layer for both the MPC and and the decentralized
PI-controller in order to save computational time. The decentralized PI-
controller uses digital PI-controllers with a sample time of Ts = 3 minutes at
the supervisory control layer to make the comparison fair.
Figure 4.25 shows the closed loop responses. The upper graph gives the
response of the top composition. The MPC scheme controls the top composi-
tion slightly more aggressive compared to the decentralized PI-controller,
==PAGE:135==
4.6. Supervisory Model Predictive Control
117
TABLE 4.18: Performance summary of the worst case sce-
nario.
Variable

0.0103
0.5167
0.0136
0.2603
xB
0.0175
0.7677
0.0533
0.5161
but does not give much improvement in rejecting external disturbances.
Note that these disturbances are unknown to the model predictive controller
and therefore it relies solely on state integrators to remove the disturbance.
The MPC scheme does realize set point changes in the top composition faster,
which is to be expected as the model predictive controller predicts that a
set point change is about to happen. From table 4.17 the IAE of the MPC
solution is about 2 times lower and the TV in the reﬂux ﬂow rate is slightly
higher, indicating that the MPC scheme did not improve control of the top
composition signiﬁcantly. In the decentralized PI control scheme the top
composition loop was designed ﬁrst, followed by design of the bottoms
composition, which was detuned until loop interaction was acceptable (see
section 4.5.3). Therefore, it is not surprising that the MPC scheme gives an
almost similar performance in control of the top composition. Figure 4.26
shows the set points that the model predictive controller instructs the PI
controllers to follow. In section 4.5.2 it was indicated that the set point of the
temperature differential controller was also an effective manipulated vari-
able for control for the top composition. Note that the MPC control scheme
changes the set point of the temperature differential controller, which assist
in controlling the tops composition, resulting in a lower IAE.
The MPC does however provide signiﬁcantly better control of the bot-
toms composition. From table 4.17 the IAE is about 5 times lower for the
MPC scheme and the TV in the reboiler is slighly higher. First, in the de-
centralized control scheme the bottoms control loop was designed last, and
therefore the top composition control loop gets to work ﬁrst, indicating a
loss of performance in the bottoms control loop due to poor handling of
interactions. From the discussion of the open loop response in section 4.5.2
it is not surprising that a coordinated action can give better control of the
bottoms composition. Both the reﬂux ﬂow rate and the set point of the
temperature differential has a similar effect on both the top and bottoms
composition. Thus, the MPC scheme balances both the top and bottoms com-
position by changing the reﬂux ﬂow rate and the temperature differential
in a coordinated way. This is a more effective way of handling the interac-
tions, compared to detuning the loop. Furthermore, from the discussion of
the open loop responses, the pressure difference between the two sections
affects the bottoms composition more, because the temperature differential
is located at the top of the column. The model predictive controller takes
this into account by simultaneously changing the set points of the both the
rectifying and stripping sections pressures, as can be seen in ﬁgure 4.26, in
order improve the control.

4.6.6
Worst Case Scenario
One of the advantages of using MPC is that it can handle constraints natu-
rally. In order to show this, the controllers will be subjected to the following
worst-case scenario:
0 ≤t ≤1.5h: The reboiler steam pressure is lost, so that it can only
operate at half its capacity
0 ≥20h: +20 % in F.
The scenario is considered a worst case scenario for the following reason. An
increased ﬂow rate of partly vaporized feed causes the bottoms composition
to approach that of the feed, that is, the bottoms composition increases
(becomes more impure). As a result, the bottoms composition PI-control
loop increases the reboiler duty in order to purify the bottoms product.
However, if the reboiler duty is unavailable due to loss of steam pressure,
the required pressure must be generated using either the compressor or the
feed pre-heater instead. Thus, logic is required to handle such a situation,
which may be provided by the operators or a model predictive controller.
This is one of the beneﬁts of using model predictive control, as it handles
logic naturally, such that operators can focus on other more important tasks.
Figure 4.27 gives the response to the worst case scenario and table 4.18
gives the performance summary. As expected, the MPC handles the situation
very well. As the reboiler duty saturates, the MPC distributes the control
action to the other actuators available. As a result, the system rejects the
disturbance in the feed ﬂow rate effectively despite the loss of reboiler steam
pressure. This is however not the case for the decentralised control system.
Here, each loop only considers its own objective and therefore this system
does not handle the situation well. As the reboiler steam pressure returns to
normal, the control system tries to recover, but as the upper saturation limit
on the reboiler duty is reached, control of the bottoms composition is lost.
This situation requires the operators to manually bring the column back to its
nominal state by generating more pressure using either the feed pre-heater
or the compressor such that the speciﬁcation on the bottoms composition
can be met.

corrupted by noise. In this work, measurement noise is modelled as being

Note that very accurate composition measurements are required to en-

PI-controllers are modelled as being digital. The sampling time at the regu-
latory control layer is speciﬁed as Ts = 1 sec. This forces the simulator to
stop every second to solve the model, which increases the computational
load drastically. The following scenario takes place:
t ≥0: +20 % in F.
t ≥20h: Increase xD from 0.995 to 0.996
Figure 4.28 gives the stochastic simulations and its performance is summa-
rized in table 4.19. The response shows that both the MPC and the decen-
tralized PI-controller can reject external disturbances and track set-points
despite measurements being corrupted by noise. Again, the MPC controls
the bottoms composition better, whereas control of the top composition is
about the same.
Previously it was mentioned that the C-MEX S-function described in
chapter 3 was required to study digital control systems. The computational
time required to obtain the MPC solution for the stochastic simulation was
9.16 hours using the C MEX S-function (MacBook Pro, 2,7 GHz Intel Core
i5, 8 GB DDR3). Using the original MATLAB S-function, the computational
time was estimated to be 718 hours = 30 days. Thus, the C MEX S-function
provided the solution about 80 times faster. A waiting time of one month
is considered unrealistic, and therefore it is concluded that the C MEX S-
function is not only convenient, it is required to study digital control systems
and provides ﬂexibility with respect to simulation times. Thus, it can be
considered a valuable contribution to the framework.

Chapter5
Conclusion
A generic rigorous dynamic model that covers different distillation column
conﬁgurations was presented and its solution procedure was analysed. The
Bisgaard algorithm was explained and it was applied to obtain a steady-
state solution of a HIDiC design adopted from the literature which was
separating a benzene/toluene mixture. One of the main limitations of the
presented model is that it assumes an ideal gas phase. It was stated that a
DAE system would result if this assumption were to be deleted, however, a
method was suggested to allow for non-ideality in the gas phase in which
an ODE system remains. It includes the solution of a rather complicated
UV-ﬂash. The method was not carried out, but was left as a suggestion for
future development work.
The rigorous model was available as a simulink implementation. In this
work, this implementation has been re-coded using the C-programming
language, and incorporated back into simulink as a C MEX S-function. The
C MEX S-function increased the execution speed of the program by a factor
of 80 and allowed the study of digital control systems. It is expected that
the C MEX S-function will save time in future projects as distribution of the
model framework is desired, and can therefore be considered a valuable
contribution to the model framework.
A hierarchical control system was designed for the operation of the
HIDiC. The design was carried out from the bottom up and was based on
the LACEY procedure. First, a decentralized regulatory control layer was
designed for stabilization of the HIDiC. The variables to be controlled at the
regulatory control layer was identiﬁed by applying engineering judgement.
The controlled variables to be controlled was determined as the inventories
in both the condenser and reboiler, the pressures in the stripping and recti-
fying sections and a temperature differential. The temperature differential
was located in the top of the column, as control of the top composition was
considered more important. The tray location of the temperature differential
was determined based on SVD analysis and it was decided to control the
temperature difference between tray number 2 and 15. Next, the best pairing
of manipulated variables with these controlled variables were determined
based on steady state criterion. Two different control conﬁgurations were
identiﬁed as feasible. In both conﬁgurations the inventories in the reboiler
and condenser were controlled using the bottoms and distillate ﬂow rates,
respectively, the temperature differential was controlled using the condenser
==PAGE:146==
128
Chapter 5. Conclusion
duty and the rectifying pressure was controlled using the compressor power
input. The conﬁgurations were different in the way that one controlled
the stripping sections pressure using the reboiler duty, whereas the other
conﬁguration used the feed pre-heater. In both conﬁgurations, severe loop
interactions was observed, resulting in a control system with modest oscilla-
tions.
Next, a decentralized supervisory control layer consisting of PI-controllers
was designed for the supervision of the regulatory control layer. A dual-
composition control scheme was considered, in which both the top and
bottoms compositions are measured and controlled. Three promising con-
trol structures were identiﬁed based on steady state performance criterion.
In all three conﬁgurations, the top composition was controlled using the
set point of a reﬂux ratio controller. In two of the three conﬁgurations, the
bottoms composition were controlled using the set point of the rectifying
sections pressure control loop, whereas the third conﬁguration used the set
point of the boilup ratio controller. The three conﬁgurations were tested in
deterministic simulations, where the third conﬁguration using the set point
of the boilup ratio controller was considered the best.
Then a linear model predictive control based supervisory control scheme
was benchmarked against the decentralized control scheme. The model pre-
dictive controller coordinated the pressures in both sections, the temperature
differential, the boilup ratio and the reﬂux ratio in order to control the top
and bottoms compositions. That is, a 5 × 5 MPC-solution. All ﬁve control
loops affected both the top and bottoms compositions at the same time and
interactions existed among all control loops. The linear model used by the
model predictive controller was determined based on step experiments, and
it was concluded that a linear model should be sufﬁcient as non-linearity
was shown to be modest after closing the regulatory control layer. The two
conﬁgurations were compared in a deterministic simulation including exter-
nal disturbances unknown to the model predictive controller and set point
changes. The HIDiC is an interactive process, and therefore coordination
of the control loops resulted in a reduction in the integral absolute error
of the control error of a factor 2 and 5 for control of the top and bottoms
compositions, respectively, at the cost of using more control action. In a
worst case scenario, where logic was required to handle the disturbances, it
was shown that the model predictive control solution was superior to the
decentralized PI-controller. That is to be expected, as logic is integrated in
the model predictive control law, and therefore handles process equipment
constraints efﬁciently. Finally, it was shown that both the model predictive
control and the decentralized control systems were robust to measurement
noise.
Although the operation of the HIDiC has been proved before, this study
proves its operability again using both a decentralized and centralized con-
trol strategy. The operation of the HIDiC is challenging due to the existence
of interactions among all loops, however, it has been shown that the system
can be controlled under realistic disturbance scenarios and the interactions
can be effectively handled using model predictive control strategies.
In the project objectives it was mentioned that there exist a need to
make the model framework more easy to use. This work has provided a
clear example of how the framework can be used with a state-of-the-art
control strategy implemented. A heavily commented matlab script has been
==PAGE:147==
Chapter 5. Conclusion
129
provided, which may aid a new user in understanding how the program
works. Furthermore, a more detailed description of the Bisgaard algorithm,
explaining exactly the structure of the program ﬂow, has been provided.
At last, a comprehensive degrees of freedom analysis has been provided,
explaining the sequence of solving the model equations while also making
it clear what variables needs to be speciﬁed by the user.

AppendixC
Inventory Control
A total mass balance around the condenser gives:
MT,Cnd
dt
= V2 −LCnd −D
(C.1)
Taking the Laplace on both sides gives
s∆MT,Cnd(s) = ∆V2(s) −∆LCnd(s) −∆D(s)
(C.2)
where ∆is used to indicate deviation variables and s is the Laplace variable.
The effect of LCnd(s) on ∆MT,Cnd(s) is obtained by assuming that ∆V2(s)
and ∆D(s) are at their nominal values:
∆MT,Cnd(s)
∆LCnd(s)
= −1
s
(C.3)
Similiarily the effect of ∆D(s) on ∆MT,Cnd(s) is given by
∆MT,Cnd(s)
∆D(s)
= −1
s
(C.4)
The effect of the bottoms ﬂow rate ∆B(s) on ∆MT,Rbl(s) is obtained fol-
lowing the same procedure from a total mass balance around the reboiler,
resulting in
∆MT,Rbl
∆B(s) = −1
s
(C.5)
The effect of the reboiler duty ∆QRbl on ∆MT,Cnd(s) is determined from a
step experiment. Figure C.1 shows the open loop step response to a step
change of 1 % in the reboiler duty QRbl. The ﬁtted transfer function is given
by
∆MT,Rbl
